To ramble further, from my skills making furniture...

Lowbrow junk: Lazy boy recliner (in its defense, very comfortable)

Highbrow junk: Perillo lounge chair (in its defense, it looks functional, non-agonizing, and doesn't make me want to back away whimpering like so many of its modern 'cousins')

Classic: Windsor chair (Light, strong, massively overdone, but it's not merely for nostalgia that the form's been around more than 250 years. I've seen examples that left my heart pounding for all the right reasons.)

My own story of the art world: I have a 'would you like fries with that?' degree and took some classes offering basic introductions to different mediums. Then transferring to another college, I tried to get into their sculpture program, but not having a prerequisite that was offered in my timeframe, the teacher rejected me. I wish I could buy her a beer to thank her. She spared me having to unlearn so much.

After graduating, I tried to build a client base, take commissions, and go to art shows. And discovered that an uncomfortable number of people had this glazed, misty-eyed look at this entertaining oddity prancing about and making pretty things for their pleasure. They had a want, an entertaining story, a fantasy, a feather in their cap, and I had a need. Money. When wants meet needs, it does not lead to an even relationship. So when at a fair, well-to-do people attempted to bargain me down with cutesy smiles, clearly entertained by the 'game' of haggling with a real artist at a real festival and I was estimating that after overhead I was making fast food wages at best, I started to hate people.

Don't get me wrong, there were wonderful people too, (and I pray that if they read this and recognize me, they recognize themselves as well) but even their sheer awesomeness wasn't enough to counterbalance the soul sucking aspects that they had no control over.

Thankfully I backed out before I burned out, and now sell supplies at a specialty shop to grumpy contractors. Grumpy contractors are so, so easy to deal with. These days, I gently decline commissions (sympathetically abstaining from cracking out an ear-to-ear freedom-inspired grin when I do so) but still make things for my own pleasure, and occasionally to spoil a family member or close friend. My newest work is called 'Watering Can' which I'm fashioning out of copper sheet, annealed refrigeration pipe, and lead free solder. Given the hours and materials I couldn't sell it and profit for a dime under $200, but it'll look mighty nice sitting on my bathroom toilet tank, and it'll keep my plants hydrated. If my sister asks very nicely, she can have 'Watering Can II' for Christmas. But I do miss the art world a little. So if the SF MOMA wants one too, I'll break my 'no commission' rule, and even drill holes in the bottom for a small surcharge of $9800.

jcummings said...
I think many of the comments here orbit around a similar theme, and I'd add mine to the fray - your post this week feels true, but is missing some pieces, or is askew in certain ways. One commenter notes that they've never met an elite or an academic in the arts who actually likes Modern Art (capital M for distinction). I couldn't agree more - it's been 40 years since "dog barf" art was de rigueur. Many art kids still get jazzed by abstract painting, but that's mostly because it's fun to make, not because it's something you can build a career on.

Your sneer at elitism is welcome, and again, feels true, but that's not why early Modern artists got into abstraction. Quite the opposite. Modern artists from the early part of the twentieth century were more generally working toward creating an art that was universally understandable. The idea that you could put anyone in front of a canvas and have a universally similar gut-level reaction was very seductive and popular. Kandinsky and synesthesia, Motherwell and war, Newman and existential angst. These guys were trying not for something that was elitist and exclusionary, but rather something universal. Andy Warhol's great contribution was to show how completely compromised that vision was because of the way Modern Art got coopted by the elite class - that in fact, commercial art did a much better job of being a universally understood artistic language.

You kind of land in the same place, but there is something important in not oversimplifying the path. The path is important, because lots and lots of thing suffer this same fate - beginning with something marvelous and true and aspirational. But you know, when you're an artist - it's not like you have much capital outside the world of people and institutions that can afford to support you, so you have gallery shows, hip after-parties at loft studios, you work to curry favor with taste-makers, and essentially, your living becomes dependent on those with money, and so your work becomes part of that exclusiveness. Warhol understood that better than anyone.

As a small farmer coming from the art world, we are very aware that the food we grow costs more than the commercial products you can find in the grocery, and so our livelihood is in danger of following a similar trajectory. It's very frustrating. Our food is definitely better, and I think worth it, but not getting swooped up in elitist cycles is hard when you want to do anything artisinal.

Spanish Fly, interesting that he drew exactly the same division!

SLClaire, no, I hadn't. Fascinating!

Steven, I didn't mean to reference the specific ideological slant of Gone With The Wind -- rather, its immense popularity among the reading public during a certain era of American history, which is paralleled by the immense popularity of Tolkien during a slightly later period. Tolkien, like Mitchell, marshalled a mass of common Anglo-American cultural tropes, some old, some very new -- consider the vibe that the phrase "the Enemy" had in the immediate wake of the Second World War! -- and used them to construct a morality play favoring a particular set of cultural politics: his were monarchist, reactionary, agrarian, hierarchical, and those pervade the plot, structure, and language of the book. That was hugely popular, especially in the US, where a lot of people have highly idealized notions about monarchy et al. Just as Gone With The Wind has nothing like the immense cultural cachet it had seventy years ago, I'm far from sure that The Lord of the Rings will have even its present clout -- much less what it had in the 1960s and 1970s! -- in another thirty or forty years. But we'll just have to see.

John Michael Greer said...
Elizabeth, if your quilts keep a sleeper warm at night and are pleasant to look at, what's not to like?

Sylvia, excellent! I've noticed that habit in many monotheists, for what it's worth -- everything has to have one overarching whatsit behind it, even when that model clearly doesn't work well.

MawKernewek, works for me -- for several years now I've considered string theory a kind of postmodernist physics, which enmacts the abstract role of being a physical theory without doing the thing that physical theories are supposed to do, i.e., produce testable hypotheses.

Sven, that's funny, in a Conanesque sort of way. Thank you. The trolls, though, seem to be staying under their bridges and grumbling to each other.

Zach, obviously I disagree. I'd start by pointing out that aesthetic experience is nonrational, and so any set of criteria you come up with is by definition an intellectual model of a nonrational phenomenon -- thus it reflects only at secondhand the phenomenon it claims to express, and focusing on criteria takes you away from focusing on the art itself. I'd point out that formal criteria inevitably lend themselves to rule lawyering -- which of course was an important part of Andy Warhol's wicked stock in trade. I'd also point out that any given proposed set of criteria will be just as contested, just as much in flux, and just as unfair as the canon it's supposed to govern -- after all, it's not being handed down by God on high the way Nestorian would suggest, it's simply the expression of the infinitely fallible taste of a particular group of people, belonging to a particular culture, at a particular era.

I suggest, rather, that the canon comes first and any intellectualizing concerning it derives from the canon, not vice versa. How does the canon emerge? By an evolutionary process to which countless individual aesthetic judgments contribute. You mention Don Quixote; that's part of the canon, and deservedly so, because millions of readers down through the centuries have delighted in it, learned from it, and treasured it. Like evolution, that process is flawed and unfair; like evolution, it results in any number of oddities -- but it's all we have to go on, because there's no other source; any attempt to legislate a canon or to impose a specific set of criteria ultimately boils down to someone's inevitably partial and flawed aesthetic judgments.

So by all means come up with whatever set of criteria reflect your own aesthetic judgments, as you've done, but I'd encourage you not to fall into the trap of mistaking the derived criteria for the experience that creates them.

Onething, er, done what?

Emmanuel, you might try some of the other buzzwords. "Abraham Lincoln" plus "critical theory" might get you somewhere.

Patricia, you will not hear a whisper of disagreement from me. Your description of the classic Mexican meal made my mouth water, and I had a very good dinner!

Hubertus, that's a very simplistic rewriting of what I said, you know.

Greg, truly said. It astonishes me that nobody draws the obvious conclusion...

John Michael Greer said...
Roseredloon, glad to hear it. Enjoy the smorgasbord!

Bill, that's a valid point, and it relates to a different dichotomy, the one between eras of exploration and eras of performance in art. To borrow Carr's terminology, the notional space of the slasher film is pretty much full up; there isn't much you can do with a straight slasher film that hasn't already been done, or close enough. That marks the end of the era of exploration and the beginning of the era of performance. Straight-up remakes are one kind of performance, but so are homages, pastiches, inversions, and the like. The sort of creative homage you've described is a great example of a constructive response to a situation in which everybody in the theater has already seen plenty of slasher films, and that becomes part of the raw material with which the artist works.

Emmanuel, I've seen serious deconstructive analyses in lit-crit that weren't all that far from what you've just done!

Patricia Mathews said...
For a good pictorial representation of the art cycle in the classical world, I suggest Thomas Cahill's TO SAIL THE WINE-DARK SEA, his pop-history of ancient Greece. The center section - look for the photo-paper chunk - runs from poctures of the earliest Archaic sculpture, on down through the most miserable of the Hellenistic period's end. After which we get the Romans, of course - half "warts-and-all" realism and half imitation Greek.

For an accessible and thought-provoking fictional view of ancient Greece from dawn to dusk, the three Mary Renault novels in my permanent collection. THE PRAISE SINGER (Simonides of Keos. "Go tell the Spartans, passers-by, that here, obedient to our word, we lie.") which STAR'S REACH reminds me of a bit. Beginnings of the Archaic Renaissance. THE LAST OF THE WINE - the great age of Socrates and Plato; Athens' short-lived empire self-destructs. And MASK OF APOLLO, in which, at the end, we meet a 14-year-old Macedonian prince and his lover, who will shake the world. Caveat: Renault has a crush on Alexander the Great, or why the only Alexander - period novel I kept was THE PERSIAN BOY. As told by said boy in later life; today he'd be considered Alexander's widow, and proud of it. Interesting. for the Persian viewpoint.

8/12/16, 5:34 AM

KKalbert said...
Hi all,
As a recovering MFA, I am loving this conversation, and I have another story about monkey art, to follow up a line of thought started by Alan S. Christenson:

Desmond Morris, a primatologist, wrote a book about apes making art, The Biology of Art, in 1963, at the height of the boom in Abstract Expressionist painting. People who took their sketchbooks to the zoo to draw the animals noticed that the chimps and gorillas seemed extremely interested in getting their hands on the art supplies. Morris looked into this, and ended up observing the artist careers of a number of individual apes. They did indeed make drawings and paintings, which had affinities to the current fashions in the art galleries of the moment.

Initially, the apes were avid to make their “art;” it was obviously a form of play that engaged them very deeply. They were fascinated by the process of making marks on paper and looking at the result. They had a clear sense of when a work was finished or not; they resented having a work taken out of their hands before they felt it was done; and they also resented being asked to continue working on something after they had decided it was finished. A development in their work could be observed, as they became familiar with the process.

As soon as the experimenters started rewarding the apes with food, to encourage them to produce artwork [maybe the market for it had become evident!], the apes began to dash off quick and desultory “works” in order to get the banana as quickly as possible. Eventually they all became disgusted, lost their interest in the activity, and refused to do it anymore.

I think this has a direct relevance to the art world in human civilization!

Esn said...
@jbucks and @JMG: I've been researching proportion in music for the past few years, so I'll be happy to recommend a few books on the topic.

The first one I'd start with is "Quadrivium" by Wooden Books, which covers number, 2D & 3D spatial geometry, music and cosmology, and the links between all of those. It is a beautiful book packed with good information (and a great appendix). I will note that of the two parts on music, the first one is more about the fundamental reality, while the second is more about the way music theory is taught in Western academies, and may be less useful (in particular, it sticks to the 12 equally-tempered piano notes). The book doesn't cover everything there is (it leans on the Greek & Western traditions), but it has great diagrams and is a great introduction.

Next, a website:
Sacred Geometry, "Proportion in Musical Scales"
It starts simple enough, but gets more complex. This article relies heavily on Maria Renold's book "Intervals, Scales, Tones and the Concert Pitch c = 128 Hz" (a good book, but definitely for the dedicated knowledge-seeker), which itself relies heavily on Kathleen Schlesinger's 1939 book "The Greek Aulos".

To hear how these different proportions sound, I recommend using the Online Terpstra Keyboard. E.g., here are my settings for the Ancient Greek Aulos modes found by Schlesinger.

From a comment I left at the "Online Terpstra" link:
---
"Apparently, there were two competing tuning systems in Ancient Greece. The one most familiar to us is the “Pythagorean”, used by lyre players, and the basis of medieval European music theory. It was based on perfect fifths [this one is described in "Quadrivium"]. A rather different one was used by aulos players, which was forgotten in Western Europe but apparently survived in many other cultures. It was based on undertones (the Greek aulos had equidistant fingering holes).

There were 7 possible modes, depending on which low note you start with, moving up. Modes can contain either the 8/14 or 8/15 degree, but not both. If your mode starts on:
-8/8 or 8/9, you must skip 8/14 as you move up
-8/14, you must skip 8/15 as you move up
-any other note, you can choose EITHER 8/14 or 8/15 to skip as you move up
-(a scale cannot begin on 8/15)

The modes are associated with celestial objects, from bottom to top:
8/8 = Saturn (Hypodorian)
8/9 = Jupiter (Hypophrygian)
8/10 = Mars (Hypolydian)
8/11 = Sun (Dorian)
8/12 = Venus (Phrygian)
8/13 = Mercury (Lydian)
8/14 = Moon (Mixolydian)
(this is the also the order of how fast these celestial objects appear to move in the night sky. See p. 304 in “Quadrivium” by Wooden Books)

The Sun mode was most common. The link above has the fundamental 8/8 tuned at 176*4=704, which allows the 1st note of the Sun mode (8/11) to be tuned at 128Hz (a common tuning back then).

Note that the Sun mode (particularly if you use 8/14 instead of 8/15) sounds like the whole tone scale of Debussy, etc. The Saturn mode sounds like the scales used in a lot of Middle Eastern music. The Jupiter mode sounds like the Misheberakh/Hungarian/Ukrainian Dorian scales.

(cont...)

8/12/16, 7:01 AM

Esn said...
(cont. from part 1)
Other books:
Joseph Yasser's "The Theory of Evolving Tonality", which has many friendly diagrams, an interesting overview of historical musical evolutionary stages (great histories of Chinese, Thai & Celtic music), and a bold "theory of everything" which may not be right, but is an impressive attempt.

"Tuning Timbre Spectrum Scale" by William A. Sethares, which, among other things, goes in detail into Bali gamelan tunings (very different from Western), and explains how you can make even octaves sound dissonant with particular timbres!

"The Spiritual Basis of Musical Harmony" by Graham H. Jackson, which I've just started reading. So far, it seems wide-ranging and thought-provoking, though perhaps over-ambitious.

"Musical Instrument Design" by Bart Hopkin. This book is wide-ranging and practical, and gives great (and often ingenious) tips for making your own musical instruments of many types with simple tools. Doing something yourself is the best way to understand sound.

Also, for those who want to delve really deeply into the subject: What Music Really Is by Bo Constantinsen (I don't know who this guy is, but he's dedicated).

Overall, "Quadrivium" is the one to start with. You can read part of it by going here and clicking on the "Harmonograph" book.

Howard Skillington said...
JMG: If you really want to look at a system of analyzing proportions in musical work, there is Schenkerian analysis. Heinrich Schenker (1868 - 1935) would have you excavate the Ursatz or fundamental structure of a composition, eventually determining its Urlinie, the archetypal descending motion which he contended underpins all pieces of tonal music.

Undertaking a thoroughgoing Schenkerian analysis of a Beethoven symphony, for example, would be to having a rewarding listening experience, as conducting an archeological dig would be to apprehending the satisfying proportions of the Parthenon.

Huck Finn - is it a classic, highbrow or lowbrow? Well, from the 10,000 ft. view I'd say it started out lowbrow, as did most of Mark Twain's books, with the characters speaking in a "common" (i.e. lower class) dialect and mostly being about kids acting as ruffians. As it turned out he struck a chord regarding race (likely by accident as opposed to design) and it was subsequently banned which gave it highbrow appeal. Now it has the misfortune of being labeled a "classic", which means kids and adults alike assume it is boring and difficult reading. I love that book, with all it's flaws, mostly for it's outright fun and authenticity.

Seth said...
@G3 McK:

While you might not need an "Academy" for a "Canon", the two ideas DO go together. The point is that a canon has to be socially constructed. The only difference between JMG's ideal canon and actually existing ones is whose opinion counts. JMG is asking for a more democratic/inclusive role for popular taste in defining a canon. But he can't get out of the circularity involved in matters of taste. There isn't an objectively True set of tastes, only what "we" can agree on. Or "what you can get away with" in Warhol's case.

Esn said...
@Avery "I think Karen has a point: new artists and modes of art were, traditionally, discovered and funded by the elites."

You should know that there is a countervailing view: that the elite art of any nation has tended to merely be a more refined version of that nation's existing folk art. For example, see Roots of the Classical: The Popular Origins of Western Music by Peter Van der Merwe. Quoting from the abstract: "From the Middle Ages to the early 20th century, Western ‘art’ music has grown out of, and drawn on, popular models. [...] Again and again simple patterns have been refined and elaborated, eventually to the point of decadence, whereupon the search begins anew for further raw folk-stuff."

This has also been the prevailing view in Russian musicology (and other fields) from the late 19th century onwards (see footnote 3 on pp. 171-172 of my thesis)

I also think that you're overlooking the huge role that technology plays in the development of new art. At least in music. Simply put: when a new musical instrument is invented, new artistic forms follow. Look at the invention of the synthesizer in the last century and what that led to, the sequencer (videogame music), the vocoder (past decade). In an earlier era, the 19th century arrival of the harmonica greatly changed American folk music. The invention of valved brass instruments greatly changed military music. In Russia, the invention of the accordion in the early 1820s-1830s, with its standardized chords and single-line melody, in due time changed the dominant character and style of Russian folk music, replacing many traits of older traditions that used older musical instruments that now found themselves on the sidelines. Kathleen Schlesinger notes in "The Greek Aulos" how the physical characteristics of the double and single reed instruments used by Egyptians and Greeks were responsible for the scales they used in their music. For example, she believes that when double-reeds were used, an octave used two different tetrachords (four-note groups) based on the undertone series, but the switch from double to single reeds allowed any tetrachord to be repeated at the 3:2 interval above the lowest note by biting the reed and making the instrument go higher. This physical characteristic of the instruments led, in due time, to the structure of the Church modes and our major scale. Another example: equal-tempered tuning of pianos allowed jazz progressions through minor thirds (which would have sounded very ugly before), at the same time as older keyboard music (using different tunings) became less attractive.

I will also note, from personal experience as a composer: when I pick up a new instrument and try to create some new melody on it, the melodies that come naturally to different instruments vary considerably.

So, if you don't like the dominant music of your era - inventing a new popular instrument that easily plays the type of music you do like would be the quickest way to change things!

Peter VE said...
Part 2
The current assignment falls right into an essay I have been contemplating for some time, on three public sculptures installed at Brown University. They don't exactly fit into your categories, but one can be both high and low brow.

The classic is the easiest: a copy of the statue of Marcus Aurelius (http://250.brown.edu/marcus-aurelius-statue). The original was on the Capitoline Hill in Rome, and served as an example of virtu in the ruler, far more honored in the absence through the centuries. When erected in 1908, I imagine a Brown education included reading the Meditations of Marcus Aurelius. More importantly, I assume the statue was remind the students that they were being educated as the ruling class of the new Empire. This was also at a time when public sculpture still had a language which was understood by most people, a language which went back nearly three millennia.

The highbrow is next: the Slavery Memorial, by Martin Puryear (https://www.brown.edu/about/public-art/index.php?q=martin-puryear-slavery-memorial). It was installed after Brown went through a public self flagellation over the source of the wealth which caused the former Rhode Island College to change it's name to honor a major donor. The sculpture is a massive ball and chain, the ball mostly buried, and the chain cut through. The surfaces are rusted steel, except a highly polished surface where the chain is cut through. The message is obvious: “We have faced up to our past sins, and we erect this memorial to ensure we never sin again.” There is an additional message in the severed chain: Brown has freed itself from the sins of the past, and can now go forth unburdened into the future. I don't think Brown wants to recognize that second message.

The last is definitely in the Warhol canon: the Untitled (Lamp/Bear) by Urs Fischer (http://www.providencejournal.com/storyimage/PJ/20160703/NEWS/160709801/AR/0/AR-160709801.jpg&MaxW=650). It is on loan for 5 years from a hedge fund manager who also was suckered into buying Damien Hirst's shark in formaldehyde. The 20' high blue plush bear (actually bronze) is leaning against a desk lamp which slices through its head. The Brown Committee on Public Art states: 'Untitled (Lamp/Bear) is from a series of works that explore what the artist calls “the inner mechanics of duality.” '. My sense is that in the duality of the artist's intentions and the purchaser's wallet, the artist will find the wallet to plunder. The only message I get from the sculpture is that study will destroy your childhood.

On a final note, the Brown Committee on Public Art doesn't give any explanation for any sculpture on the campus installed earlier than a Henry Moore sculpture from 1963.

Looking around my office my stuff on the walls runs the gamut from a couple of prints that would fall into the motivational poster category, calendars cut up and the artwork side framed (great way to get prints of stuff you could never afford in real life...), some nicer things we got at the Woodland Arts Fair (Lexington) and the St James Art Fair (Louisville) which are both juried, a few pieces by local artists. The items that I have that are highbrow-ish I only got by accident, so to speak. The wife of one well known local photographer was a colleague of mine on the board of a non-profit. The other, Margorie Guyon (national and international recognition) gave me a large oil on canvas piece and several small prints at ridiculously low prices because I set up her bookkeeping for her and kept it straight for a couple of years.

I pick things because I like the colors, basically. That's it.

In the low brow arena: My boys enjoyed a steady diet of Mel Brooks, Monty Python and Weird Al growing up...which I don't think was a disservice at all. I think it gave them a good foundation, believe it or not. Of course, regarding music they also listened to a lot of Vivaldi, Handel et al, Celtic & folk, movie soundtracks, "new age" instrumentals, and ethnic music of all sorts from around tbe globe - especially middle eastern / Sephardic, and yes, klezmer.

They saw all the classic and popular sci-fi and fantasy movies, and we have an extensive library of classic children's books and adult non-fiction (Economics especially).

Speaking of soundtracks, go to Pandora online and search for "skyrim elder scrolls" and what you will get is a channel that plays movie soundtracks and video game theme music that is as good if not better than lots of the stuff played by modern composers at the local universities. It pays everything from kitchy Raiders if the Lost Ark and Legend of Zelda to cinematic Lord of the Rings type works. If you like techno music, try the Tron channel or the Daft Punk channel, and you'll hear things like classically trained violinist Lindsay Sterling playing with an electronica band... Nowhere near high brow but great to listen to while cleaning house. When I need something soothing so as not to consider bludgeoning clients who brings me a big mess to fix, Secret Garden or Enya works great. To hear my favorite soundtrack piece at the moment, go to https://m.youtube.com/watch?v=xhJTMeytTWw

None of this other than the classical music is high brow of course. The Vivaldi channel plays Baroque era music of all sorts, and that is one of my favorite channels. I have numerous CDs as well for when the internet gets too expensive. I do wonder what will happen to music in general - the younger generation won't pay for it or buy CDs anymore. Not even cover charges at local pubs, as a friend of mine in a local band told me. And the bar owners don't want to pay the bands any reasonable fees, either. It does cost money to buy instruments, and time to come music and practice. Local bands are having a hard time here. The one big local venue, Thursday Night Live, only plays bands that do covers of popular music. They can mix in a few original pieces, but they have to be the same style as the covers. There is no venue, really, for non-rock/pop music at all except an Irish pub that sometimes played Celtic. The isn't even a jazz bar anymore! Even the local opera house had succumbed to showing mostly modern productions, not classics or anything in the classical style. It's a wasteland out here.

LewisLucanBooks said...
Dear Mr. Greer - OK. One last quote from Timberg's "Culture Crash: The Killing of the Creative Class." And, no, I don't have any connection with the author or own stock in the publishing company :-). It's just one of those chances that the book resonates so much, given this weeks topic.

"For all the ups and downs of culture and the creative class, an old classical idea, dressed in different vestments each century, persisted: that drama, poetry, music, and art were not just a way to pass the time, or advertise one's might, but a path to truth and enlightenment. At its best, this was what the middlebrow consensus promised. Middlebrow said that culture was accessible to a wide strata of society, that people needed some but not much training to appreciate it, that there was a canon worth knowing, that art was not the same as entertainment, that the study of the liberal arts deepens you, and that those who make, assess, and disseminate the arts were somehow valuable for our society regardless of their impact on the GDP. Today, every one of those statements goes against our post-industrial, technocratic grain."

Another book worth looking at for anyone in a "creative" position, budding or established, is JoAnneh Nagler's "How to Be an Artist Without Losing Your Mind, Your Shirt, or Your Creative Compass: A Practical Guide." (2016).

And, off topic, but you're always saying how reasonable the real estate prices are in your town (Cumberland, Maryland?). Well, I finally got around to taking a look. Oh, my gosh. Wonderful older homes for $20,000 or less. Property taxes of $25 or less? How do they do that? If I were younger and not as well established .... It looks like an ideal place to live, especially if you have some kind of independent source of income. Lew

Ed-M said...
Hi JMG!

Yes, I have compared the two. The last of the best seemed to have been made in the 3rd Century. To the "cubist" representation, those were truly exquisite. But modern day art-critics would call the "cubist" version to be ex-qius-ite!

Now I have my own list of examples of highbrow rubbish, classic or canon, and lowbrow trash.

Sculpture:

Highbrow: Jala and Dinos Chapman, Great Deeds Against the Dead which trashes Goya's Heroic Feat! With Dead Men! utterly, by using department store dummies to represent his masterwork in three dimensions. And it has a sequel, which is completely gross.

Classic: Greek, Roman, Renaissance, baroque, Neoclassical and Victorian sculpture. Examples are: Julius Caesar, 1st C. bce, Michaelangelo's David, Donatello's David, etc.

Lowbrow: Those cheap plaster or plastic statuary that can only be found in mom-and-pop Italian restaurants (the good kind, not the pizza joints) in working class neighborhoods. Also the god-awful dreck in Madame Tussaud's Wax Museum and the like, and also in Caesar's Palace, Las Vegas. Are we sure it isn't TRUMP Caesar's palace???

Domestic Architecture:

Highbrow: Philip Johnston's glass house in Connecticut.

Classic: Monticello, Mt. Vernon, Beacon Hill town houses.

Lowbrow: Split-entry or raised ranch homes (what another commenter called split-level ranch houses), US urban row-houses built for the lower classes in the 19th Century and are now falling into ruin.

Town Planning:

Highbrow: Le Corbusier's Radiant City, Frank Lloyd Wright's Broadacre City.

Classic: Welwyn Garden City, colonial Savannah, colonial Philadelphia, Washington DC, some New Urbanist towns that have thrived like Seaside, FL.

Lowbrow: The endless 19th C. US city grid, typical subdivisions with "ant trails" EVERYWHERE!

Pop Art

Highbrow: Richard Hamilton, Just What Is It That Makes Today's Houses So Different, So Appealing? It's a collage, really, from the 1950s but it hearalded the Pop Art that was to come in the '60s by its use of imagery from lowbrow pop culture.

Classic: Roy Lichtenstein, Crying Girl. It was Roy's work that essentially defined Pop Art.

Lowbrow: Early newspaper comics, comicbooks (before they became graphic novels!)

Propaganda/Advertising:

Highbrow: Wang Guangyi (Harbin 1957) Great Criticism - Coca Cola (green). Combines the iconic logo with Socialist Realism, ex., Soviet icons holding Mao's little book.

Classic: The Coca-Cola logo itself. 'Nuff said.

Lowbrow: Early Coca-Cola advertising. One ad features a female in a bar, holding a glass of coke aloft, whilst a hand with the cheque appears from beyond the left edge of the panel. Ghastly and poorly executed!

Phew!

8/12/16, 1:13 PM

Renaissance Man said...
Ah, one of my favorite topics. I work just a 10 minute walk from the AGO in Toronto, so I spend a lot of lunch-hours there on my unlimited-access membership. It makes all the difference in the world to be able to spend a whole half-hour gazing at a single cabinet of objects, carefully examining each one, or a single wall of paintings, or even a single painting. And no TV screens. So I've already been doing your homework, as it were.
I bathe in the masterworks of the 12th, 13th, 14th, 15th, 16th, 17th, 18th, 19th, & early 20th Centuries and then, after about 1910, most art degenerates into something visually ugly. Apparently wealth is no immunity from gullibility.
I decided when I was very young that modern art was crap and nothing since, not being 'educated', nor being exposed to lots of it, nor yet having it 'explained' to me, has caused me to change my opinion. Stephen Pinker's book "The Blank Slate" explained how this doctrine produced, amongst many other aspects of late 20th Century life, "...art without form or beauty, and architecture without decoration or human scale..."
I had some glee a couple of years back when it was revealed that the Tate and most modern galleries that pushed this crap into the world were secretly funded through proxies by the CIA as anti-soviet propaganda to demonstrate how 'free' we were in the west versus the 'controlled' (i.e. tasteful) art behind the iron curtain. That's where the money came from to pay outrageous sums for piles of tires in a public square. I giggled a lot over that.
Books and writers like Tom Wolfe's "The Painted Word" helped me conceive of why I appreciate the art and formulated my own, idiosyncratic idea of what qualifies as art.
Art requires three things: first, it requires knowledge - knowledge of the materials and medium should be evident in the work, what works, what produces a desired effect. Second, it requires discipline - the discipline to learn the craft, to learn to use the tools, to develop ability evident in the skillful use of the tools. As you say, anyone can throw a sponge of paint at a canvas and produce a splatter, but to apply paint with a brush to produce a particular effect requires the discipline and patience to learn how to do it well. Third, art requires some imagination and this is where the whole art-theory business gets bogged down and sinks into the morass of self-delusion that characterizes modern painting - smears of colour or paint-by-numbers canvases or 'found objects' glued to an old board - as "art." Just because it is imaginative does not make it art, art requires evidence of the other two pieces of the tripod.

Eric S. said...
First, last month’s homework report: I’m still working on Rushdie in the background, though I think I still managed last month’s homework. Based on the back cover and forward, I decided that given Rushdie’s background, I probably wouldn’t understand most of the author’s polemics on religion without having much more familiarity with Islam than I do, so I read the Qu’Ran. There were enough moments of slamming it shut and having to go do something else from all the passages about polytheists and how they’re going to hell to fit the bill (although there really wasn’t anything in the entire thing worse than the sort of stuff that you run across in the writings of the average Great Awakening figure). However, there were other aspects of reading that made it quite rewarding. The poetry was beautifully composed, following a gorgeous spiral ring structure, And the richness to the lore and storytelling, as well as some of the laments, prayers, and praises scattered throughout, and I was particularly intrigued by the extra dimensions that the presence of the Djinn led to the cosmology. It also actually (contrary to what one might expect from the way our culture views Islam,) is much more geared towards inter-faith cooperation than modern Christianity is, since the world it’s emerging from is the multi-cultural world of the early byzantine, and treats the other monotheistic religions of the region as equals so long as they follow the teachings of their prophets and treat Muslims with respect, and despite the condemnation of pagans, it still insists on treating them with respect so long as they behave honorably, with the only actual encouragement of violence being within the context of defense and retaliation.

8/12/16, 2:21 PM

Eric S. said...
Now, on to this month’s topics:
One thing that’s giving me some difficulty with this week’s piece is a certain difficulty I’m having with labeling anything short of the tackiest of kitsch on one end, or well… something like this: (http://www.cbc.ca/radio/thisisthat/new-york-artist-creates-art-that-is-invisible-and-collectors-are-paying-millions-1.2847219), as trash.

I think in that sphere, the concept of notional space takes on a very important role in the trashy art discussion. Going back to the discussion on Wagner in Retrotopia a few weeks ago, you had Carr expressing the point of view that one of the great things about Wagner was that he experimented and pushed boundaries while there were still boundaries to push, and it worked. The more notional space gets filled within a cultural form, meanwhile, the more closely the likelihood of achieving success through experimentation resembles the likelihood of winning the lottery, so that you eventually go over the top into the sort of self-referential nonsense that we’re discussing: (https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=KG16ZjxJTMw.)
However, there’s a lot of grey space in the middle where there’s some room for personal taste (to continue on the opera thread, for instance, I’ve noticed that Phillip Glass is a polarizing figure with some people liking his work and others seeing it as falling well into the pretentious trash category). Meanwhile, I’m under the impression that within the fantasy genre, you’d probably classify Harry Potter as lowbrow trash, while other people see it as solid popular literature, and still others may regard it as a potential future classic that may one day stand alongside the Oz books. My impression is that most modern literature falls into this category, divided between the high art and the popular entertainments of a culture.

Within that, though, there’s a wide grey area between kitsch and Warhol, a lot of that trash may fall within that realm of on one hand entertainment that might never be high art, and might even be trashy to some people, but that’s still solid entertaining reading, and on the other hand a lot of work that might not be highbrow trash, but might nonetheless be something of an acquired taste that looks an awful lot like highbrow trash who those who aren’t interested. And the classics, and canon of a culture come out of both realms, mostly decided by the process of cultural selection that we call legacy. Perhaps I’m still thinking about the same sort of thing but just having something that is giving me a knee-jerk visceral reaction of calling anything trash unless it’s infamously bad, or falls into the categories of smut or hate speech. Final thought: it seems to me that the low-brow clichés also touch onto the level of consciousness that folklore comes out of, and is very close to the soul of the culture. Which means that it’s really the low-brow trash that sows the seeds of the future.

Anyway, as for my homework reading (I’m not sure I can bring myself to calling anything trash, but I will go with generic lowbrow pop literature, and high brow experimental literature), I think I’ll go with the sword and sorcery fantasy genre. My three books will be:

A Terry Brooks Shannara novel as my low brow
A Michael Moorcock Elric novel as my high brow
And Well at Worlds End, by William Morris as my classic.

I actually haven’t read any of those, despite one being the classic that kicked off fantasy literature as a genre, one being the series that created the generic pop fantasy genre (read: low brow trash), and one being the series that shook up generic fantasy fiction and kicked it in an experimental direction from the author who helped kick off the New Wave movement (read: high brow trash).



8/12/16, 2:26 PM

Jason Fligger said...
"I'm not interested in a definition of art. I'm interested in encouraging individuals to develop a clear personal sense of aesthetic and intellectual taste." I have thought about your response in the context of a drive through my detroit neighborhood where a lot of defacement has occurred and where signs of decay are everywhere. Then I asked myself, is this ugly? Why do I live here? When I began to think about what I was looking at in a way that separated what I was looking at from adjectives such as "decay" "disorder" "defacement" I began to see it in a different way. By asking us to develop a "clear" personal sense of aesthetic and intellectual taste, are you asking us to freeze our perception? Doesn't aesthetic evolve in response to associations between what we see and what we feel? While admire the technical skill (craft) of the dutch masters, I personally find very little artistic value in the portrait of some dead member of the aristocracy. All that tells me is that the artist was a skilled prostitute working for the wealthy and advantaged. He really didn't have anything of value to say about the living conditions of the time. In contrast, consider the early modernist critique of art and architecture and their association of decorated building facades with aristocracy and facism. They sought to get to the basic truths-the primary colors of the DeStijl movement were a failed antidote to Hitler who hated their work and much preferred the works of the classicists. Obviously, modernism has also developed its own cult followings and has evolved into various styles that are often followed blindly. The true genius of art is usually found in the avante garde who are constantly pushing the limits and trying to tell us something about the world we live in. Isn;t it ironic that the DeStijl movement died when it became a style? This is the destiny of true art. Once it has delivered its message, it is time to move on to a new language yet to be discovered. This will drive a shift in aesthetic. As a soon to be architect (one more test to pass before I obtain my license), I find it interesting to listen to commentary on building styles. What I take away from these discussions is that that style does not have as much impact on how someone feels about a building as what they think. The essence of a good building seems to hinge more upon how it feels-scale, interaction with the street, interaction with light, shadow etc. have far more impact on building perception than applied ornamentation. Perhaps these are the "universal truths" that can help us to develop a mature aesthetic but I would argue that they are only about as influential in aesthetic preference as DNA is to our behavior.

If I understand correctly, you are suggesting that the natural state of a human is to live within a single culture and that this allows for social cohesion and a unified aesthetic opinion.

John Michael Greer said...
Karen, thank you and so noted. It would have been completely out of character, though, for Peter Carr to have expressed himself in that kind of qualified fashion -- if you'll glance back to other episodes, you'll find that he always speaks in that sort of crisp generalization, sometimes insightfully and sometimes not. One of the requirements of my art, as a writer of fiction, is to make sure that characters speak in their own voices rather than in mine.

With regard to a Retrotopian canon, you're right that I should develop that further in the direction of arts; I've focused on technology and the habits of everyday life, with a fair amount of attention to architecture and a nod or two to music. As I revise the posts into a book, I can certainly include more hints about the visual arts -- there will no doubt be paintings hanging on a variety of walls in Toledo and elsewhere, and those could unquestionably be used to sharpen the point of the book. Glancing back at the state of art in the Atlantic Republic might also be useful. You doubtless know the current art scene better than I do; can you suggest some soulless, ugly corporate art that would make a good starting point for the sort of thing I did with the Atlantic Republic Capitol?

Finally, I agree entirely that an election year is the best possible time to pay attention to things other than the political spasms du jour. Perspective is crucial, and art's a good way to get it.

Hammer, explore your annoyance. You may learn something from it.

Lynford, the human mind functions by means of simplification, reducing the complex curves of existence to a set of lines simple enough we can understand them. Them's the breaks. (Of course it's also relevant that these weekly posts are essays rather than books...)

Varun, no argument there. I think a lot of people use it because it allows them to avoid being sneered at.

Zach, and as long as criteria like yours are meant as a response to aesthetic experience rather than a replacement for it, I have no objection at all.

Lewis, I'll definitely get that book. As to how Cumberland has real estate prices so low, simple: it's got half the people it did in 1970, and supply and demand took it from there. The one caution I offer people who are thinking of moving here, or anywhere else in the small town regions of the Rust Belt: you have to bring your job with you. There are very, very few jobs here, and if you take one, a lot of locals will resent you for depriving someone local of an income.

DaShui, that was quite a marathon show! I'm glad you enjoy it.

UFCMGP, it strikes me as unconvincing. Revolutionary new artistic ventures happen whether or not there's an elite funding them -- if anything, they're more common outside that environment. Think of the birth of jazz: very much the hothouse environment, but it was a hothouse defined not by vast amounts of money, but by a politically and economically marginalized racial community pursuing its own musical idioms in the teeth of the mainstream. For that matter, I'm quite sure Schoenberg would have kept at it in his basement if he hadn't gotten elite funding, and maybe the need to find someone who wanted to listen to his music might have inspired him to put his undeniable talents to use in less sterile directions.

Pygmycory, in a sense, The Lord of the Rings is fan fiction based on the Silmarillion -- Tolkien was his own first and biggest fan, and the mythology of the Elder Days was something rather more important to him than a literary creation -- so I think you may be on to something. As for the band pieces, good -- will you consider doing the assignment with a different, and possibly less familiar artistic medium?

John Michael Greer said...
Ed-M, phew indeed!

Renaissance, nicely done. Your threefold description works, to my mind.

Eric, of course there's a lot of variation and wiggle room -- as I noted in an earlier comment, since all we have to go on is the accumulation of individual aesthetic experience, one person's trash is another's masterpiece. I do think Harry Potter is going to end up being quietly forgotten, like so many other once-popular children's books, but time will tell; that's one of the reasons why a canon should consist entirely of the work of dead people. With regard to your fantasy trisection, though, you might want to pick up an Elric novel or two before redefining that end of Moorcock's oeuvre as highbrow trash. It's the pulpiest of pulp fantasy, gloriously trashy stuff -- he really should have been able to go back in time to get it serialized in Weird Tales with gaudy Margaret Brundage covers. I say this, by the way, with immense affection; the Elric saga, and even more so Moorcock's Swords trilogy, are among the books I mentioned in the post, the ones I go back to and read when I want to enjoy a good solid trashy fantasy. No, if you want highbrow trash from Moorcock, you have to go later on to his Jerry Cornelius novels, which are splendid examples of the genre in its florid decadence.

Jason, no, I'm not asking anyone to freeze their aesthetic perceptions; I'm asking my readers to enlarge their sense of what's already been done and develop richer and more sensitive aesthetic palates. If the only thing you see when you look at a Dutch master is a dead aristocrat, by the way, first of all, you haven't looked at a lot of Dutch masters; the majority of them were painted for a middle class market and include a wealth of really beautiful scenes from daily life. Second of all, if your politics have blinded your aesthetic sense to the point that that's all you see even if the subject of the painting does happen to be a dead aristocrat, then this discussion probably isn't going to make sense to you anyway.

As for your comments about the "natural state" of human beings, that's a cheap shot, as I think you're well aware. The natural state of human beings, of course, is to grow up in one culture and then to interact with many others; that's as true of hunter-gatherers as it is of today's Europeans, say. It's precisely in the interplay between a thoroughgoing grasp of the achievements of one's own culture and the unexpected insights and creations of others that we get some kind of sense of what it means to be human.

Candace, it's an exquisite piece. I wish he'd devoted more of his time to such things.

One exhibition featured enormous canvases (5'x10'?) painted all in black, with a paint that had a very thick texture (think brush-on roofing tar, not rolled latex). We dutifully wound our way through the serpentine gallery, one black monolith after another. Hmm... My 8-year old son got a turn or two ahead of us. I found him standing wide-eyed and still, opposite yet another monolithic painting, but this one was intensely blue. "It just presses me against the wall, Dad!" I think he was having an aesthetic experience.

This is gonna to be fun. Assuming no budgetary limitations, a well rounded soul sucking corporate art collection should include:

3 dimensional work/installation/video

For their edgy ick factor: Damien Hirst: any dead, sliced animals in formaldehyde; Tracey Emin: any of her soiled mattress installations(which don’t overtly show too many sexual bodily fluids)

For minimalism so sucked dry of life why bother: Donald Judd: stacked aluminum frames with colored plexiglass; Sol Lewitt: rectilinear geometric shapes made of square steel tubing painted black; Joel Shapiro: curvilinear twisted square tubes of metal painted black trying to look not too geometric;

For edgy minimalism that would require a Starchitect designed space to house it: Richard Serra: enormous, free standing, several meters long, curvilinear towering shapes of rusting metal that feel like they could fall and squish you at any time, even better if they block the natural pedestrian flow and irritate a lot of the workers.

For serious, somber rusting steel figurative sculpture: Antony Gormley

For edgy exasperating video: Marina Abramovic: any of her videos(bonus if they last longer than 5 hours and show her staring at someone staring at her)

Because painting has been declared dead for the past 10, 25, 50 or one hundred years I will submit it separately.

If you haven’t heard of it, Artsy.net is a website and good resource that is easily finds any kind of art quickly. You type in a name or genre and it gives you images of your search or similar examples.

Kyoto Motors said...
I truly am sorry to be so late in the game on this one. Missed out on last weeks discussion, or else I would have been fron and centre here, no doubt.
anyway, here is my long-winded response. I hope you have the time to read it and reply. I know after the 200+ mark that's rare, but here goes nothin'

I will first off, acknowledge that what you are touching on here is necessary, as the art world does indeed need a rethink. But before I get on board, I’d like to say that your parable of the two tailors/ would-be painters is a crass oversimplification of things, even if some of the criticism is warranted…
Unlike in your fairy tale, Abstract expressionism didn’t just come out of nowhere. Nor did it emerge from a disingenuous desire to pull the wool over the eyes of the public or simply make a quick buck. So I have to call foul: just not fair to dismiss a collective cultural exploration of the emergent, expressive and esthetic possibilities of painting as such.
Disclaimer: I am an artist. I am wholly invested in that identity, and make no apologies for it. I am accustomed to both praise and support, as well as scorn and derision. I have come to learn that my art is not for everyone, and that should not wish for it to be otherwise. But I do think that investing in the understanding of art history, and where these ideas (like Brillo boxes or “dog barf”) come from, will potentially expand one’s awareness and perception of things. That is at least my aspiration as an artist.
I guess what I’m saying is that there is an important distinction to be made between the pretenders and the genuine, sincere artist’s work – even if it happens to be indistinguishable at first glance.
But am getting ahead of myself.
I think the real problem in art today, as is the problem in many other spheres of life, is the industrialisation of those things that have a traditional foundation. It’s a real conceptual stretch to connect the dots between a fresco by Giotto of the early Renaissance and a “post-modern” sculpture by Jeff Koons. And yet, the art establishment likes to weave a narrative history that does exactly that.
Industrialising art has taken many forms, and started – as one would expect – early on during the industrial revolution. The best known early triumph was probably Impressionism, which emerged from a host of new observations about what painting could do – in part spurred on by the advent of the photograph. But another factor was the new industrial middle class, and the cascading effects of the new socio-economic order. Impressionism was scandalous from the perspective of the old-guard academy. But the artists were well schooled by that very academy, and proceeded, nonetheless, to dismantle the phobias, taboos and neurosis of the establishment. Only the most dogmatic of the old order were unconvinced.
And then, as waves upon a shore, the new generations emulated the paradigm established. And they received support from the emergent industrialists who embraced the innovation and the avant-gardism that reflected their own ideological biases.
more to come...

Even if the new art is good, it gets lost in the flood. It just seems inevitable that things have to go the other way, which is a bit depressing for someone who gets a lot of joy from writing new music. But maybe in every historical period there is a lot of new art, and time just filters out the best from the worst, and anyway I'll continue to write music because I simply like doing it.

Also, several commenters have recommended The Mandibles by Lionel Shriver - I went out yesterday and got a copy, and although I'm only 60 pages in, it connects very well with the ideas you post about, so consider this another recommendation!

@Esn: Thanks a lot! I will get a copy of Quadrivium to start with, and keep those other books in mind. Would it be correct to say that Quadrivium is the one most suited to applying the concepts in practice? Because I'm interested in experimenting with proportion while composing my own music.

One thing I noticed with many of the titles you suggested is that they seem to apply mainly to scales and 'vertical' proportion. I had a look for books that dealt with proportion in rhythm and large scale form (ie, proportion used in composing pieces 'horizontally') and I did find a book which analyzes Debussy's music to see how he followed natural proportion (Debussy in Proportion by Roy Howat). I will look for a copy of that, too.

@Howard: Thanks! None of my books however really go into detail about this kind of analysis directly, so I don't know if this is really the case or not?

Synthase said...
I'm not particularly sure how the model of low-brow vs high-brow applies to scientific research, either it's trash or it isn't.

But here's an example of a classic in molecular biology:
Singer SJ & Nicolson GL. (1972) The fluid mosaic model of the structure of cell membranes. Science 175:720-31 retrieved from: http://life.umd.edu/cbmg/Faculty/song/688D/Paperdiscussion/Singer%20and%20Nicolson%201972.pdf

It's well worth the read -- it's chock full of the reasoning and evidence behind something you'll see presented as just so in biology textbooks.

John Michael Greer said...
Eric, if you want a really good example of Moorcock in his pulp-fantasy phase, the one I recommend is The Swords Trilogy, which you can get in used paperback these days for three bucks and free shipping from Abebooks.com. Elric is rambling and to my mind rather labored; Moorcock wrote the first version of it when he was quite young, and though he revised it thereafter, it's still an early work. To my taste, the story of Corum Jhaelen Irsei, the Prince in the Scarlet Robe, shows his mature pulp style at his best. Kyoto, of course it's a crass oversimplification. Every parable is. I'm also pleased to see that my post has launched a spirited discussion in which dissensus is the order of the day. John Michael Greer said...
Dennis, I can certainly second irritation at seeing tax dollars spent on dog barf of various kinds! With regard to industrial arts, that's a real challenge, as it's been part of the definition of art for many centuries now that to be art, something has to have no practical use. The exclusion of traditional women's arts such as quilting and weaving from the status of fine arts, which they eminently deserve, has been justified by the fact that quilts et al. actually serve some purpose other than decoration. The industrial arts fall afoul of that same difficulty, despite which there are objects crafted by industrial designers that are, to my mind, as elegant as good architecture (the one art that does get to be useful).

Jbucks, that's a massive question. How does an artist collapse now and avoid the rush? Of course one option, if you've got a steady income, is to do the same sort of cost-cutting and skill development as everyone else, but there's also the question of art media -- does your art require high technology to produce or display? What are its raw material requirements? Can you keep doing it as the economy slides? Those are major questions; I'm still contending with some of them in my own art as a writer, and I have no easy answers for other arts.

Patricia, funny. Japanese culture has a remarkable ability to borrow aesthetic ideas from other societies and take them further than anyone else, and so it doesn't surprise me at all that Japanese kitsch is kitschier than anybody else's kitsch will ever dream of being.

Synthase, no argument, that's a classic. For highbrow vs. lowbrow trash in research, though, I think a case can be made for the distinction. Lowbrow trash research is the sort of bottom-feeder stuff that proves some meaningless detail of something, purely for the sake of getting an article published. Highbrow trash is the sort of overblown stuff that's so busy being cutting-edge it forgets to engage in actual science -- I don't know if that's gotten into molecular biology yet; you might check out string theory in physics for a world-class example.

Synthase said...
I'll agree with this definition: "Highbrow trash is the sort of overblown stuff that's so busy being cutting-edge it forgets to engage in actual science"

I'd take issue with your definition of low-brow trash research, "the sort of bottom-feeder stuff that proves some meaningless detail of something, purely for the sake of getting an article published" however. If it's done with rigour, that meaningless detail is likely to be a very small but important piece of someone else's puzzle, and likely took up five years of some sla-, er, PhD candidate's life. It might not be a gem, but it's the sort of diamond dust used to drill into the unknown. Not trash.

Trash research is where, purely for the sake of being published, an article with some serious methodological or statistical flaws (or worse) gets through.

That seems to lead to high-brow trash in the sciences being that which fails to be science due to excessive conjecture, and low-brow trash being that which fails to be science due to insufficient competence.

For my example of high-brow trash in biology, I won't cite any specific article but instead a trend towards in-silico studies which threaten to uncouple themselves from actual living systems.

For my example of low-brow trash in biology, I'll put forward this example of a retracted, yet famous and much politically abused article linking Roundup and GMO corn to tumors in rats:
Seralini et al. (2012) RETRACTED: Long term toxicity of a Roundup herbicide and a Roundup-tolerant genetically modified maize, Food and Chemical Toxicology 50:4221-4231 retrieved from: http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0278691512005637

Esn said...
@JMG "I wonder if you've considered writing a good general book for the nonspecialist on proportion and the like in music"

Thank you for the encouragement, but I'm afraid I don't feel as if I know enough yet! I know enough now to see the flaws in much of what I was taught, but not yet enough to have formed a new synthesis to replace it.


@jbucks
You're welcome!

"Would it be correct to say that Quadrivium is the one most suited to applying the concepts in practice?"

Depends what you mean by "in practice". "Quadrivium" is great for getting the ball rolling creatively, and it has some great diagrams that I often consult. If you're working with modern software at all, "Tuning, Timbre, Spectrum, Scale" would be my next pick. If you're more interested in acoustic instruments, pick up Hopkin's "Musical Instrument Design" (some instruments, e.g. kalimbas and harps, are easy to retune to different systems). Once you have a good grasp of the maths, play around with different tunings on the Online Terpstra Keyboard I linked to (e.g. on a tablet). Maybe buy an isomorphic keyboard like the former Axis-49, or build your own (the piano layout isn't great for experimenting with different pitch systems).

8/13/16, 4:24 AM

Esn said...
(cont. from previous post)

@jbucks "One thing I noticed with many of the titles you suggested is that they seem to apply mainly to scales and 'vertical' proportion. I had a look for books that dealt with proportion in rhythm and large scale form (ie, proportion used in composing pieces 'horizontally') and I did find a book which analyzes Debussy's music to see how he followed natural proportion (Debussy in Proportion by Roy Howat)."

Well... after all, a musical note is just a rhythm that's too fast for us to hear as individual beats (faster than 16 beats/second or so). I can't think of anything really good about rhythm/musical structure off the top of my head, though the book you found seems interesting. A number of the composers at my university did talk about using the Golden Section as a structural principle.

I will however highly recommend doing analysis yourself if you can. Find some pieces you like, find their scores (or listen to them, and make your own sketch), and analyze the structure. Analyze the elements that seem important to YOU (these will probably differ depending on the type of music you're studying). Compare different pieces (from the same tradition, or between different traditions) to see what's the same and what's different. I did that in my thesis (linked in my previous post), and have generally found it more productive and liberating than using someone else's analyses. It was also recommended to us by many of the professors, and for good reason. Sure, we were taught a number of "standard forms" used in classical music, but only by actually analyzing things yourself do you notice all the nuances.

In a lot of instrumental folk traditions, there isn't a structural form for the "whole piece" as such - rather, somebody may start playing something softly in a free rhythm, then get bolder and play a recognizable melody of fixed length in steady rhythm, then other musicians may join in as the same melody (or several) repeats over and over (but sounds a bit different each time, as different musicians vary different things), then the original musician may or may not call out "CHANGE!" and start a different melody, and eventually one of the repetitions will be the final one by some sort of signaled or unsignaled consent of all the musicians. If there are people dancing to the music, that will often determine how long the music goes on, based on the energy in the room (and the "CHANGE!" may start a different sequence of steps). In these cases, maths doesn't enter into it so much, rather the musical structure is organic and intimately tied to the environment (although there's a certain repetitive element at the heart for practical reasons, both for the musicians--some of whom may be learning melodies new to them as they're playing--and the dancers).

8/13/16, 4:27 AM

Darryl Kubian said...
Having been involved on both sides of the trash heap for the past 30 years in the classical music world, this week's post could have easily described the trajectory of Western classical music for the last 100 years. With the advent of purely chromatic harmony at the end of the 19th Century and the rise of serialism with Schoenberg et al., composers on the highbrow side have tried for most of 20th century to divorce themselves from the physicality of the overtone series. This vain attempt to completely intellectualize how we compose and listen to music thankfully seems to have failed, largely I believe to the lowbrow sides insistence that diatonic harmony just sounds better. This is not to say that I completely reject the techniques and aesthetic that can be achieved from "modern" musical compositional frames, as I studied with serialist Charles Wuorinen. My objection is that in most modern classical music composing scenes, nothing is valid unless it makes a break with the past and is obtuse in some way. If the audience doesn't like it, too bad. That's progress. If I had adopted that attitude in my own music for TV or film, I would have been out of a job very quickly. Let's also not forget that many of the greatest composers in our Western musical history worked "for hire" and still managed to create great works of art - sometimes quite challenging for their audiences, and other times instantly beloved. For me, it still comes down to whether it's good or it isn't, whether you like it or you don't, in any time period and area of the trash heap it is on. It's all compost anyway...

Pedro Campobelo said...
I must agree with some of the comments above that while you certainly did capture a relevant aspect of modern art, your approach is perhaps a bit hurried and unfair.

I've never thought that the early Duchamp "masterpieces" were supposed to be serious. The impression I got from reading the Dada magazines was that they were pretty openly like the con artists from your parable, at least in the early years. They were making fun of the art establishment and it looked like it was a competition to see he could make the stupidest or weirdest thing be considered art.

Many modernist movements, like surrealism, seemed to be sincerely trying to popularize new ways of engaging the world, it never seemed to me that being recognized by the affluent was in their agenda.

My own country's modernism interpreted the modernist revolt against tradition as revolt against what was felt as foreign, European tradition that could only be appreciated by the affluent. They were trying to create new forms that were adequate to the sounds, colors, landscapes and peoples of our country. Accordingly they delved deep in folklore, fauna, flora and history of the country. Much of what they have done is still popular today, though Academia's favorites are the ones that better emulated the nonsense coming out of Europe at the time.

I used to be a modern art enthusiast until I had to read Sophocles's Oedipus Rex for a philosophy course. That little book shook my tastes and worldview for good, I'm now very far from being a friend of modernism. I still think, however, that the general problems with modern art were not the ones presented in your article.

You can't make this stuff up - highbrow and lowbrow trash in one object.

I think we all agree that the thin, clinging, flimsy, low-necked, cotton/poly (at best) T-shirts made in China and sold in big box stores count as lowbrow trash.

Classic would be the sturdy all-cotton conventionally cut T-shirts - found all too often, alas, only in the Men's or Young Men's departments.

This item was on sale on a small garment rack at the back of a store I'll call Whole Paycheck. The design was similar to that of the big box trash tees, but so are many fashionable ones these days.

Caryn said...
@onething:

Thank You for your reply. I agree, as others here have noted, that visual art, like all art is a form of communication. I don't personally think it therefore HAS to be a collective experience. Some art is, some can be experienced alone. Sam Sheppard once described his plays as springing from his own "personal mythology". That description stuck with me because Yes! We do all have our own personal mythology - we bring our our past experiences and feelings to every new thing we encounter including works of art. While some are collective, a whole group of people have personal mythologies close enough to have the same reactions to a piece, and yeah, that's a great experience, Those are the works that will likely endure through time; However, IMHO that does not mean that a work that induces a solitary individuated reaction is totally worthless.

In terms of the meaning you get from a representational painting vs. the blank you get from abstract art, (I'm talking specifically about those blobs of color works), How do you feel when you see 'abstract-art' in nature? Some weird elaborate texture of lichen, or twisted tree bark, flowing colors of mineral deposits in a rock or white grey and pink clouds? For me, these little gems of beauty induce a similar reaction to a blobs of color-abstract art painting. They're just nature at work, not pretending to be anything, not requiring an explanation, yet they are fascinating, and do sometimes remind me of something else or induce a strong feeling or memory. I am free to take away from them whatever meaning hits me.

This is totally different, IMHO than a representational artwork of a giant teddy bear cleaved by a lampshade that purposefully evades understanding or meaning. I can certainly appreciate another art-troll or artist doing something playful, weird and whimsical, non-sequiter and random, but IMHO - that is not appropriate for Public Art. Public Art kind of needs to be a collective experience. That's the whole point. An expensively funded and permanent piece in a college square is not the place for just some weird whimsy or internal, navel-gazing experience, (much as I love navel-gazing experiences.) IOW: I don't get it, and I don't think we are meant to 'get' it. AND it is too specific to cop to the 'take-from-it-what-you-will' explanation. So this and your other examples are the finest examples of High-Brow Trash.

The pile of rusting metal junk in the fountain - The most charitable explanation I can come up with is that the artist was trying to recreate the natural artwork I talked about above - just glorying in the shapes, textures, lines…. Walking around an actual junk yard, I have definitely been utterly captivated by the random accidental sculptures of piles of twisted metal. Maybe that's where the abstract artists fell into a trap- it IS inherently fascinating when you come across natural or accidental weird beauty. It feels contrived and trollish when purposefully recreated. (?)

But it's also likely a genuine case of JMG's 2 tailors shilling dog barf.

Space Seeder said...
Karen: I've been thinking a little bit in response to this statement you made:

I believe you confuse my frustration with the critical writing about the art as a defense of the art. Not so. Binary pronouncements of good/bad, like/dislike aren’t helpful to artists. Tell us what you like, want, etc. I mean the nuts and bolts: colors, subject matter, paint styles, whatever and why. Gives us some idea of where to start from, very few art schools are teaching that now days. We may not like it, but I promise we listen.

My own view of art that is 100% non-representational is that it doesn't give someone like me who is not educated in the arts anything like a starting point in determining what the meaning is. Sometimes the title helps, but usually I'm lost from there. It's not that I don't like flashy colors in and of themselves - I do. It's just that I think that particular style aspires to a meaning that it doesn't achieve.

So I guess what I'm trying to say is that there has to be a strong representational element in order to achieve meaning. It doesn't have to be Rockwellian or anything close. I dreamed up a couple of examples this morning, and please forgive me if these already exist and I've unwittingly drawn them from my memory rather than my imagination. Want to say something about the objectification of women? Paint a picture of a frontally nude woman, neck-to-navel, with the breasts replaced by eyeballs and the collarbones shaded to suggest an angrily furrowed brow. Want to say that nature always wins in the end? Paint a picture of frightened robber barons sinking into a shrub-lined fissure in the earth, which on closer inspection appears to resemble a vagina. Want to say the opposite? Paint a picture of the blue-marble Earth on the floor, encircled with contemptuous robber barons ashing their cigars on it. You could call it "Climate Bukkake".

You might not like my particular examples, but anything in that form at least gives a place to communicate. Maybe the representational elements can be more subtle than that. My examples are pretty in-your-face with the message. Whatever. You're the artist - season to taste.

BTW, a few years ago I made three dog barf (I'll give that term a rest now) videos of a few minutes each and uploaded them to youtube. They didn't mean anything. I was just playing with the medium. I can't find them now, as I abandoned that account, but if I could have, I would have linked to them on here so I could have shared in the criticism.

Scotlyn said...
Art is a funny thing. I am always making things, but, true to my very low-church upbringing, all made things must be practical. I would have said the subject of art is one of which I am ignorant and have nothing useful to say.

And yet the home I share with my husband, who left school at logarithms because the tables were awkward to work with after chores with only the tilly lamp to see them with, is full of paintings he has paid small amounts for over the years, always direct from a local artist because it was something he liked. He even commissioned (traded a woodstove he'd come by in a different trade) a painting of the now roofless and falling down cottage in which the logarithms put paid to his formal education.

My two sons, one not terribly artistic, one very much so, were excellently taught by a gifted art teacher who helped both to "stretch" beyond their comfort zones. Although the artistic fellow produced a ceramic piece I still find slightly disturbing...


Earlier today I got a lift home with a 60+ neighbour who loves building replica stone cottages, and has enrolled in a carpentry course this winter because, well, she's a maker.

Making is something I profoundly respect. And yet, I still think of myself as having no aesthetic sense at all...

Kyoto Motors said...
Of course Progress is woven into the whole Modern Art narrative. How could it not be? And you could argue, what with the achievements in abstract expressionism and minimalism, painting has been all the way to the endpoint of that linear narrative, straight to a sort of “post-modern” crisis of purpose. I think in the best cases, an awareness that the narrative is non-linear has emerged.
On the darker side of things (in the market place) the “cutting edge” of artists are now reflecting not the ideology of progress (the old paradigm of capitalism), but the ideology of financialisation and fraud (the new paradigm of post-peak capitalism). So many people are in the art game for the money, even though only a handful can sustain a real income over the long haul…
