And now a Family Guy version of the Emperor's New Art has popped into my head! The child who goes up to the painting is none other that Baby Stewie, who says it looks like dog vomit. The artists, the committee members, and the Emperor all looked down their noses at Stewie and said, "Child, you obviously know nothing about art."

So the child said, "Fine. I'll ask someone who does. He turns his head and calls out to Brian, the family dog, who walks up, holding a martini (they serve drinks at these functions as I'm sure you all know). Then Stewie pops the question: "Brian, could you give me an educated opinion about this contemporary painting?"

Brian looks at it, sniffs it, and says, rubbing his chin. "Hmmm... it's a fine exemplar of the Borborygmist School of expressionist painting." Then, with a very traumatized look on his face, exclaims, "Which means it's MY dog vomit!!" He snatches the painting away, and he and baby Stewie walk out of the exhibition with the rest of the family, carrying the objet d'art with them.

On their way out, Stewie asks, "Brian, how did you know the name for that school of painting?"

Brian replies, "Simple. Before Peter adopted me, my owners' last name was Borborygmi."

"It passes, but so does a sunset, a flower. Should aesthetic pleasure be frozen in time and space? into an object?"

As someone who has always derived the great majority of my aesthetic pleasure (joy, ecstasy, feelings of sometimes overwhelming beauty) from the natural world, an integral part of the experience is the beautiful bitter-sweetness of being well aware of the transient nature of the event.

'Art' can be taken as short for 'artifice', and though I do find an occasionally compelling painting or such, and I love music, and good literature, and am intrigued by the emotions they all can invoke, I could live with the Real World (vs. the Virtual World). So much of the Virtual World, or Culture, or what have you, smacks of control-freakery to me. Freezing an emotion, holding onto a feeling, controlling the experience, or maybe just assuaging boredom (though control-freakery practically guarantees boredom, ha ha). But...

You can't hoard sunsets, or thunderstorms (we just had a beauty!)(here comes another!), or those 5 minutes when the mist is coming up in the dripping forest with the sun gleaming and beaming through it, or the sparkles of color from the the sun playing through the tiny prisms of a fresh snowfall... sorry, you gotta keep going out to see, can't own it, can't buy and sell it... just like you can't say 'hrmmm, that was a great meal, guess I can stop eating now.\

OK, it's Saturday night and I'm rambling. I could ramble on, but I'm signing off and enjoying the rain and thunder...

8/13/16, 6:02 PM

JoAnna said...
I'm sorry to be coming in to this very interesting topic so late in the game, but I'll dive in with a few of my thoughts anyway, and do my best to catch up. (*ahem* Hopefully in the right thread this time. *sigh*) Two points:

1.) As a professional violinist, I perform quite a bit of "new" music as well as the standard war-horses, and I heartily agree with most of the article. I also feel that the "artists" in your Emperor story have done a great deal of damage to culture by discouraging people from having their own opinions. By not allowing people to own their own appreciation or lack thereof for art, in some ways it destroys art's legitimacy and place in society. If everyone feels that they just don't "get" art, why should they go to a concert or art gallery? When it seems their opinion isn't informed enough to matter, they often chuck ALL of it in to the dustbin, and say they just "don't like classical music" or art galleries, or whatever. VERY frustrating for me, when honestly, there's just a lot of badly performed, badly presented (and particularly in the case of modern music, badly written) classical music out there. (I think with shrinking money and opportunities in the arts, especially here in the U.S., there's also something of a circling of the wagons effect -- we're all trying to band together and not disparage each other, since we know how tough it is out there.) There is a huge variety of styles and time periods to choose from, and just because someone hears a smattering of a Mozart symphony in a crowded train station and doesn't like it, doesn't mean they couldn't have a profound spiritual reaction to a vibrant, outstanding live performance of a Mahler Symphony or Brahms Requiem.

However, there's also the larger educational and contextual problems in our culture, as you've been pointing out in these posts. If people have no experience evaluating ideas, or lack the time and emotional ability to focus in on their own reactions to artwork, would a great performance of Brahms or Bach even make an impression on them? I go back and forth on that quite a bit.

2.) This also ties in with the issue of the "future" of artistic careers that another person brought up. Maybe the Western classical canon is just playing itself out (sadly) and the art of the next time-period will be something completely different and speak to people in another way. From a descent perspective, my husband and I often talk about folk music and chamber works being the most likely to survive and thrive in the music world. Plus, what makes something great "art" can be so many different things to different people! (The difficulty of your canon definition is that it requires an institution and a specific cultural framework to exist within, of course. My email signature is a quote from a 1967 documentary about Sister Corita Kent, "We have no art. We do everything as well as we can.” I think it's an idea from Balinese culture, but I cannot verify that at all.) I also teach violin privately on the side, and my colleagues and I have all discussed the moral dilemma of working with young people who may be considering making music their career. If I had children, I certainly wouldn't encourage them to consider the standard Symphonic or other "normal" performance career route as their future. They have to be aware of how the world is changing, and understand that it might be an even more challenging path than usual.

Caryn,

"However, IMHO that does not mean that a work that induces a solitary individuated reaction is totally worthless."

No, of course not and we all do experience even very specified representation art individually to some extent. Well, we experience everything individually.

"In terms of the meaning you get from a representational painting vs. the blank you get from abstract art, (I'm talking specifically about those blobs of color works), How do you feel when you see 'abstract-art' in nature? Some weird elaborate texture of lichen, or twisted tree bark, flowing colors of mineral deposits in a rock or white grey and pink clouds? For me, these little gems of beauty induce a similar reaction to a blobs of color-abstract art painting. They're just nature at work, not pretending to be anything, not requiring an explanation, yet they are fascinating, and do sometimes remind me of something else or induce a strong feeling or memory."

Well now, that is different. Nature at work IS something, the processes are real and are always within their own natural context. Perhaps if you framed a piece of lichen or some splattered raindrops on a rock, it wouldn't hold much interest either. The beauty of it is what it is part of and that one can discern its place within a larger, active reality.

Thanks for your take on those 4 horrors. Very sensible.

John Michael Greer said...
Unknown, far and away the worst meals I've ever been served, bar none, were incredibly expensive nouvelle cuisine at nose-in-the-air restaurants I only entered due to weddings et al.: ornate presentation and desperately precious ingredients, but the food itself was bland, dull, and profoundly unsatisfying. The last time I got fed something like that, I went out afterwards and found a decent burger joint where I could get a good bacon cheeseburger and a good dark beer. So I won't argue with you a bit!

Scotlyn, interesting. I wonder whether the same distinction -- trash involves dependence on complex outside inputs, while art does not -- might be applicable more generally.

Synthase, it may vary between different branches of science, then. When I was first at college, I took experimental design and statistics courses through the psych department, and I also knew some of the guys at the computer lab who helped the grad students commit research fraud -- the usual gimmick was to do some kind of more or less pointless experiment, run statistical tests on it until you found something that was statistically significant at the .05 level or better, and then write the paper as though that's what you were trying to prove. Since you can be sure of getting at least one apparently significant factor at that level by pure random chance if you run at least twenty different statistical analyses, a great deal of junk science got accepted that way. I also got to hear, though, of the more common gimmick in that department, which involved finding something sufficiently irrelevant that nobody had bothered to look into it, and making that the center of your career, running endless variations on the same basic experiment, and never quite conclusively proving anything: the claim was that tenure committees et al. paid attention to the number of publications, not their relevance. That's been my touchstone ever since for lowbrow trash science.

Esn, may I offer the advice of a somewhat experienced author? Nobody ever knows enough to write a book. The only excuse for writing is that you know a little more than the people who want to read it, and can communicate something to them that they want to know.

Darryl, thank you! The notion that something can't be genuinely good unless people don't like it strikes me as one of the most destructive beliefs in the contemporary arts scene -- and of course that includes all art, from music to architecture and beyond. If artists would start challenging that, I think a lot of the barrier that keeps art from receiving support from the public (and selling artworks to the public, thus enabling artists to support themselves) would go away in a hurry.

Bill, exactly. The Broadway musical was a very rich art form with a somewhat limited notional space, due to the constraints on what you can do in a Broadway theater with orchestra et al. At this point we're well into the performance era, and as you point out, the only really innovative things being done involve fusing the musical genre with other genres -- and how many times can you do even that before it gets stale?

Pedro, I certainly don't claim to know enough about the history of art in your country to claim that my narrative applies there. Here in the US, for that matter, there have been very serious and well-intentioned efforts in the modernist end of things -- but there has also been a vast amount of more or less deliberate charlatanism and fraud. I suppose it may simply be that since colonial times, the United States has been the happy hunting ground of the con artist, the snake oil salesman, and the sideshow fake!

8/13/16, 11:03 PM

John Michael Greer said...
Robert, pretty consistently, the artworks that are praised to the skies by the arbiters of contemporary taste vanish without a trace when the era is over, and the things that go into the canon are things that were despised by the purveyors and consumers of highbrow trash. The great critical success of the 1811 literary season, for example, was Mary Brunton's novel Self-Control. Heard of it? I didn't think so. Another book published in 1811 was Jane Austen's Sense and Sensibility, which was panned by the highbrow critics as mere popular entertainment. You tell me which one entered the canon!

Patricia, thank you. I needed a good laugh.

Wbricex, hmm! Thank you; it hadn't occurred to me that laws could be approached as an art form, but you make a good case.

Scotlyn, this notion that "art" is something that only the upper classes have, and the lower classes don't dare claim to have, I see as one of the great tragedies to unfold from the current insistence that art must be repellent and useless to be serious. I'd like to see people like you reaffirm their capacities to create beautiful things, and to value those things as art. As for aesthetic pleasure being frozen into a single enduring shape, not at all -- how about music, dance, theater, or the other performing arts? Should cooking and eating a meal be considered a performing art? I'd be in favor of it....

Kyoto, fair enough -- and I suspect that the financialization has a lot to do with the marketing of dog barf these days.

Ed-M, very funny, though I don't know what a "Family Guy version" might be -- is this a TV reference by any chance? One correction, btw -- "borborygmus" is the technical medical term for gurgling in your stomach, the sort of thing that you hear from a dog's belly before it throws up. ;-)

Zach, and your voice also counts. You enjoy things that others consider highbrow trash? Fair enough; that's your right, just as it's their right to loathe it. Full disclosure: I had a college housemate who had to read Shikasta for a class, and hated it so much that when the quarter was over she tore it up and burnt it page by page. This was a woman who normally loved books. My feelings aren't that strong, but honestly, I find them unreadable. Your mileage may vary.

Onething, I dance on toes. Mine, and occasionally others'. ;-)

Chris, well, there you are. I wonder if abstraction becomes more prominent as societies isolate themselves from the natural world.

JoAnna, exactly. I understand the circling the wagons effect, but when it's paired with the insistence that nobody has the right to dislike any artwork that has been endorsed as fine art within the arts world, the result is exactly the effect you describe -- people walk away from all of it and seek refuge in popular culture, however trashy, because there they're allowed to express their opinions freely and avoid things they don't enjoy.

With regard to the survival of the arts in the Long Descent, that's a huge issue, and one I'm still trying to suss out. Obviously some arts and some styles are much better suited to survive than others; if your violin students can change gears and play fiddle music in a popular style, they're much more likely to be able to put food on the table -- but what happens to the classical repertoire if country-western is the only thing anyone wants to hear? I don't have the answers yet; down the road, I plan on discussing that in much more detail, though.

jbucks said...
@JMG: Funnily enough, in The Mandibles, one of the characters is a novelist who stopped writing because she could no longer make a living from her novels, because not only was there too many novels being written, but no one was paying for them. This happened before the collapse depicted in the novel, and as the collapse continues to unfold (over a few decades), people are too busy trying to survive to pay much attention to art.

My guess about art in a future of long decline is that art-making becomes a hobby for most people (folk art), the few professional artists who remain will either make art for a much smaller upper class who still have some time to enjoy art and money to pay for it, or they will make lowbrow trash. As you wrote, if the audience for lowbrow trash are poorer people who desire the 'reassurance of the familiar', then in a world where more people are poor, then the audience for lowbrow trash will grow. On second thought, though, the poor by definition won't have the money to pay for lowbrow trash.

I wonder if there are historical precedents, I don't know what happened to artists as Rome went through its decline, for example.

By the way, to answer your questions as regards to my own art, I used to create electronic music until a few years ago. I was actually a pretty good 'synthesist' and user of music software, but I sold it all, and put all the money into piano and composition lessons. It didn't feel right to be making music using energy-intensive electronic music hardware and relying on machines to do the work for me. Now all I need is a piano, a pencil and some blank manuscript paper, although I do still use notation software for convenience.

@Esn: Thank you very much once again! I'll get a copy, that sounds worthwhile to explore.

Yucca Glauca said...
JMG, I'd like to add in my thoughts for your consideration.

I think Retrotopia has the perfect amount of art-related content already and doesn't need anything more.

I'm a millennial, and the majority of millennials I know more often frustrated by new technology than pleased by it. They're quite happy to point out all the ways old technology worked better and even complain about Progress in the abstract. The problem is that such insight is usually followed by a sigh and a resignation that we can't go back. I think that these crowds would be quite receptive to Retrotopia as it is, but I worry that they might turn off their brains and start snarling if you make art a more major part of the book.

Ironically, the crowds I have in mind usually swoon over older aesthetics and would happily nod along with Carr's comments. The thing is, the defense every millennial I know has developed against highbrow art snobs is to insist that, in fact, everything really is art and it's completely a matter of individual preference and if you try to tell them what they can and can't like then obviously you're the one who doesn't understand art.

Of course, you're not trying to dictate what they can and can't like, but I fear that too much of a focus on art might be interpreted that way. Peter's occasional comment about visual art and the specific genres of older music he likes strike me as just right to engage people who like retro fashion, but too much might make them suspicious of an art snob agenda on the part of the author.

BillTheSlink said...
I just wanted to relay an encounter with "art" that was in vogue when I was a young teen. I happen to be of an age (40) where in order for what anyone with any sense would call pornography would be considered protected "art" if it had what the Supreme Court had termed "Socially Redeeming Value." I remember when my cousin and I got our hands on such a vide as curious thirteen year old boys are apt to do. We watched this film with as much interest and excitement as you would expect from two young teenagers who had never seen such a video. After ninety minutes of three or more couples having wild conjugal relations, the last couple to be featured got dressed and walked outside through the door of the hotel where this was being filmed. The man lit a cigarette and starting puffing. The woman turned to him and said, "You shouldn't smoke; it causes cancer." The man said, "You're right. I'll quit," and snuffed it out on the sidewalk with his foot. That three second exchange made the preceding ninety minutes "protected art" covered under the First Amendment according to the high court and not pornography.

8/14/16, 3:43 AM

Sven Eriksen said...
Quote JMG:

"[T]o what degree is our old friend the religion of progress involved here?"

I wonder if maybe one of the ways we can approach that is to compare it to the way we are conditioned to enact our lives as a kind of microcosm of the myth, because that is perhaps where the content and structure of the myth is on display in its crassest terms. When the devout undertakes his personal journey of progress, that is supposed to be a linear ascent towards more and more of two easily recogizable features: The first is the movement into increasingly larger quantities of dysfunctional abstractions and the manipulations thereof, and away from doing anything useful, valuable or productive. The justification for this is that the abstract structures one is creating is a kind of ultimate reality of which the real world is but an uneven and flawed reflection, and that this will allow the latter to be more effectively manipulated. Given that the flight to abstraction is really a form of nay-saying in the strictly nietschzean sense, the opposite is actually the case, and thus the abstract must be isolated and protected from the world it is supposed to represent, as that world will gladly clobber it and its creator given half a chance. This is supposed to be accomplished simply by insisting that said world (and the dwellers within) are stupid, really stupid, which is what the sneering directed towards those on the outside is about.

The second feature is that the flight away from doing things that are useful, valuable or productive must be rewarded with a steadily increasing ability to disproportionatly consume the fruits of the labours of those who are not engaging in that flight themselves. The justification for feature two is extrapolated from the last part of the justification for feature one and ends up circling back to mean that the dysfunctional abstractions and their manipulators are what enables anything useful, valuable etc. to occur in the first place. For a first rate example listen to economists try to explain why it is the existence of economists that enables the economy to function...

Officially appointed artists peddling dog puke for absurd amounts of money is just one more form of acting this pattern out, though in a perhaps slightly less obvious (and arguably less harmful) way.

Glad you liked the video. In these difficult times we need the occasional reminder to stay true to our barbarian selves ;-)

JoAnna said...
JMG,
I'm very happy to hear you'll be discussing art in the context of descent in the future! I'm definitely looking forward to hearing your thoughts on it. For myself, I see it as (potentially) a return to a situation similar to times before the Industrial revolution -- namely, patronage. Before the rise of the middle class through the Enlightenment/Industrial revolution etc., high art was created pretty much exclusively for the upper classes who had the leisure and resources to enjoy it. (Either via the Church or the imperial courts.) It was only in the 19th century that "classical" European music began to be available to more people, leading to the rise of performer-stars like Liszt and Paganini, and the ability of composers to write for larger ensembles in the increased capacity of the larger concert halls, built to accommodate new middle-class audiences.

All this is to say that as the middle class and the "leisure" time afforded by cheap energy goes away in western civilization, it makes sense to me that classical art (whatever it is by that time) will shrink back to serving smaller audiences again... For my students, that means far fewer opportunities (though I'm hoping not a complete lack of them!) and perhaps training more in the apprenticeship style of the old days, rather than the mass-produced musicianship of conservatories. Being in my 30's and happily supporting myself as a classical musician, it sadly feels I just "slipped in" before the bell as it were, as I've seen the freelance scene in the NY area dry up considerably in the last few years. From what I hear from my older friends, it's a completely different world from the heady days of gigging in the 70's, 80's, or even the 90's.

Pinku-Sensei said...
@JMG: "drum and bugle corps (corpses? How do you pluralize that?)" Both the singular and plural are spelled "corps," but the s is silent in the singular but pronounced in the plural; the word is French and the original rules sort of followed along. Coincidentally enough, a corps played on that very confusion by calling their show "The Drum Corpse Bride." The program could have been very pretentious, but it ended up being a great crowd pleaser.

As for the same patterns playing out in drum and bugle corps, that's definitely been the case since the 1970s, when the corps themselves wrested control of the activity from the veterans' organizations, who did their best to keep it lowbrow trash for wage-class youth. As soon as the directors, instructors, and judges got control of the activity, they did their best to make it more artistic, which meant that pretentious trash was the eventual result. They also converted into an activity for salary-class youth, which the wage-class alumni have resented. As you can see, more than one of the societal trends you've decried has occurred in the activity.

@Karen: As a resident of Michigan, I've been following Art Prize. I wasn't surprised at the institution of a separate juried prize from the popular vote, as the opinions of the masses generally do not coincide with the cognoscenti.

AT said...
Part I:
First, belated congratulations, JMG, on the ten year anniversary of the ADR. I have been reading for eight years, but it took me a long time to figure out how to comment. (I discovered accidentally that one can comment if one has a gmail address—hope this may help someone else.) I very much appreciate the weekly dose of sanity and provocation and the tight control of the comments that make this an endlessly fascinating read.
On to art: I agree that art needs to communicate and would go further and say that it needs to communicate from spirit to spirit. Of course there are techniques of composition and materials usage that assist this process greatly, but I don’t think that they are the sole enabler of this type of communication. Paintings can be technically accomplished without saying much (Watteau, anyone?) I think that one of the main problems with modern, and especially contemporary art, is the emphasis on the intellect, on making a “statement”, which leads to the problem of the art being solely self-referential. I have been very fortunate to see a fair number of the west’s great art works as well as a fair amount of dreck. Here are a couple of examples of what I mean.
In an exhibit of contemporary works by new artists, there were two rather poorly made pottery plates mounted on a wall where they looked random and out of proportion. The card enthused that the plates could be hung in different positions for an infinite number of works of art. Oh, wow (not). The lack of proportion did not make me see the space in a new way; the plates did not communicate anything other than ugly plate-ness. In contrast, an exhibit at the Clark Institute showed contemporary works by an African artist made out of bottle caps wired together. These large, colorful, shimmering pieces were breath-taking. They said something about beauty from waste and ugliness, but they were also just plain beautiful, speaking to my spirit about nobility and resilience (and something beyond words). They were dimensional pieces, and a DVD playing in a side gallery showed the artist talking about his works, and especially about how he enjoys collaborating with curators about how the works are to be hung and shaped. He can see new things in his works through the curator’s eyes. Yet the pieces come out of his inner vision, his feelings about the society in which he lives, its heritage and current problems.
At another contemporary exhibition, an enormous pile of hard candy was heaped on the floor in a corner. I cautioned my (then) small child not to touch it. However, on reading the card it turned out that we were supposed to help ourselves to the candy. The artist would weigh what was left at the end of each day and top it up to the precise weight at which his partner had died of AIDS. I think this may have been 89 lbs. Now this is a horrifying weight for a grown man, but it is not a small weight of candy. The candy was just a large pile of candy. It communicated nothing to us about love or loss or the horror of an epidemic even after we had read the label. It obviously meant something to the artist, but something so private that he could not share it in any way that would touch another person. We could intellectualize at length about the symbolism of candy or of eating, but in the end, nothing of the artist’s experience or emotions was shared. Was it art? I’d say that it wasn’t.

8/14/16, 2:01 PM

AT said...
Part II:
Of course art is best appreciated in person, as the discussion on the Sistine Chapel illustrates. I could have cared less about Van Gogh’s work until I saw it in person. Amazing! Absorbing! I consider prints of his “Sunflowers” to be ugly. The painting itself seems alive. A modern work at the art gallery at Princeton University is a canvas painted black. (Sorry I can’t remember the artist or find him on line.) Look across the gallery at it and it appears to be a high-priced scam. Up close, one can practically fall into the painting. It induces a kind of peaceful, meditative trance state. It more than passes the “could I have done it?” test. I most definitely could not have. Anything I painted black would look like the side of a (black) barn. The same museum is loaded with warhol, including several actual Warhols.
As for low-brow stuff, it usually communicates something—that’s why people like it. Take the much-maligned Norman Rockwell. He was clear that he did not consider himself an artist, but an illustrator. Nevertheless, his works are technically accomplished and cause folks to chuckle or heave a sentimental sigh, and what’s wrong with that? In the case of Elvis on velvet, it is probably the reminder of a favorite song sung in his gorgeous voice that makes people want him on their walls (post-ironic hipsters of course want the kitsch of it all).
To follow up on the comments on Picasso, I think he is over-rated. “Guernica” is a work of breath-taking power. The rest of what I have seen—not so much. My father used to say that Picasso was just “having us on”, and IMHO at least some of it seems to be sly warhol.

Violet Cabra said...
When I was younger, growing up middle class in suburban massachusetts I was in love with highbrow trash. The dadaists and surrealists captivated me with their secret symbolic language. later, I found that learning human languages is way more satisfying.

As I've aged I've thought less and less about art; most books I read are non-fiction, and while I'm clearly informed by aesthetics I've let that part of myself atrophy somewhat. I don't commonly listen to music or look at visual art either. This is in large part from being turned off by the aesthetic extremes of art that you anatomize. I began reflecting a bit on this more after reading Dostoyevsky's the brothers karamazov and being deeply moved on a deeper, symbolic level, that most art doesn't reach in me.

I have, however, put a great deal of time recently into studying herbalism, talking with folks about the herbs they use and feel that I can characterize different uses in the framework you describe.

Vernacular Herbalism (low-brow): the use of culinary herbs for healing minor ailments. Eating a clove of garlic for a cold, taking ginger tea with honey or drinking warm milk before bed are all time honored rituals that have important chemical effects. They are accesible and comforting. I do not consider them "trashy" but they are vernacular.

Kitsch: buying bagged tea which tells you how to use it. The yogi tea that says "smooth move" for constipation, "cough formula" "sleepy time" or what have you. The important part of this classification in the comfort that the tea provides (I say this with compassion and understanding - I drank endless cups of these teas microwaved as a child) and the minimal level of thinking that is needed on the part of the consumer.

Elite Herbalism (high-brow): the use of adaptogens that come in the form of capsules. People buy ashwagandha or holy basil or eluethero in capsules in combination with other herbs. The people who buy them are, in my experience come from class backgrounds which dispose them towards being high brow. The elements of cognoscenti are present here as well. So while the use of adaptogens is totally valid and I use them with gusto in my practice, I find that their use can often be correlated with elitism, and the sort of obfuscation that you mention as an important aspect of high brow trash.

Warhol: gemstone essences. I have great respect for homeopathy and flower essences. This includes the more spiritual aspects of healing with herbs as well. There is however, it appears to me, a level of warhol in the making of essences of gemstones, or tincturing coyote blood, or capturing the full moon in a bowl of water. While I want to acknowledge the spiritual power that using these sorts of preparations can have, there is a certain high brow absurdity that starts to come in, especially if this is the mainstay of someone's practice. Again, I see many good parts to herbal warhol and it works a lot, but as when perceived as an aesthetic statement, it starts to look silly to me.

Canon: Traditional herbal systems, especially with the practitioner crafting their own preparations. The sort of beauty of giving someone a tincture I made of reishi or a nettles vinegar is hard for me to convey. Gifting a little bottle of lemon balm tinctured in brandy likewise in an aesthetic experience of the highest degree. The materials, preparation and presentation are always taken into account, and especially with formulas, there is the dance of traditional forms giving rise to distinct and personalized meanings. Also, I agree with David Hoffman who writes that when you have a variety of herbs that are appropriate and indicated, why not choose the best tasting one?

8/15/16, 7:45 AM

ganv said...
Thanks. That is a good bit of common sense that doesn't get written down too often. When reviewing scientific papers, I often use these categories although not in those words...the lowbrow and highbrow trash as well as the soon to be classics. I haven't yet run into much kitsch or warhol that claims to be science, but the Ig Nobel prizes and many commentaries on scientific sociology often make for good entertainment. There is a way that science is somewhat different than art, in that there is a utilitarian aspect to good scientific measurements and theories. One of the challenges is always that in addition to the obvious lowbrow and highbrow trash, there is a large amount of useful but somewhat lowbrow work by people who did some interesting work but don't have the training, talent, or didn't invest the time to do it really well. And there is an even larger amount of useful but somewhat highbrow work by people who did some interesting work and are trying to pass it off as more important than it is. It feels to me that one of the most useful contributions these days is critical synthesis that extracts from the vast scientific literature the enduring insights. Traditionally that is done in review papers, but increasingly the review papers avoid saying anything negative and simply summarize the mind twistingly complex array of partially contradictory results that have been published. How do you characterize the professional caution that causes many to avoid conflicts of ideas because it is the most effective career development strategy? (It also happens to require the least thinking, so it allows publishing of many papers per year.)

Chris Smith said...
I like abstract expressionism and so will defend it. For me, the point of abstract art is to observe two things. First, your visceral reaction to the piece. Second, the narratives or stories your mind wants to impose on what you are looking at with the realization that they are in your mind and not in the piece. There is a meditative quality about it.

18th and 19th century landscapes however ...

Which brings me to post 19th century philosophy (a subject I once taught). Here are my votes.
Lowbrow Trash: Ayn Rand, Ken Wilbur, and Richard Rorty
Highbrow Trash: Marty Heidegger, Ludwig Wittgenstein (the "Tractatus" years)
The Classics: Jose Ortega y Gasset, Antonio Gramsci, and Alvin Goldman

Charles DeYoe said...
I first want to offer kudos to JMG for this post inspiring me to look more closely at my own taste in media.
My interests vary wildly: the first two homework assignments consist of a large portion of what I read of my own devices, this time though... well, trying to pick apart what makes something "highbrow trash" is certainly posing a bit of a challenge. Most people I know aren't especially concerned with older works and so I feel like even a Marx Brothers comedy I'd hold up as a classic might come across as "highbrow trash" among people who think all black & white movies are inherently boring.
In terms of visual arts, I can understand the concept easily. I visited MoMA in New York City a while back for a Paul Gauguin exhibit (which I loved) and found 90% of the other works in the museum to be pure trash.
I suppose for literature, James Joyce would be an example of highbrow trash. Ulysses is a book that' I didn’t enjoy reading but has stayed with me. A lot of what might count as highbrow trash really resonates with me. I assume William S. Burroughs would be an easy example but when I've read (and re-read) his books, they always inspire me to gleefully cackle with a certain indescribable mania. I suppose Harlan Ellison (who famously walked out of an interview when they called him a science fiction author) is an example of highbrow trash in sci-fi... but the RAW EXPRESSION and SHEER MADNESS in his work is just something I find wonderful.

Thinking about movies... well, Last Year at Marienbad is a critical darling that I'd be hard pressed to imagine a normal human being actually *enjoying* or understanding, but it is a beautiful and evocative film nonetheless. I’m not sure I want to rewatch it for this homework though.
Looking at genres first, horror is one of my favorite but horror movies tend to be absolutely hated by critics; to the point when a horror movie that turns into a critical delight (Psycho, Jaws, Silence of the Lambs) gets labeled as "psychological thriller" so it doesn't have to come across as anything as trashy as a horror movie. But Psycho, Jaws, and Silence of the Lambs are also crowd-pleasing movies with mainstream-appeal. It’s tough to find a horror work that’s also a darling of critics.
Maybe I should look at comedies... are Woody Allen movies classic or highbrow trash? Even at his worst and most self-indulgent, I normally find his movies to be entertaining.

So I'll turn to comics because I read a lot of those: and I doubt there's a better example of highbrow trash than Neil Gaiman's run on The Sandman. Among comic fans, it's routinely held up as one of the greatest works of comic-dom (critics have argued that it's REALLY a "serialized graphic novel"). The story concerns a group of immortal "don't-call-them-gods" godlike-beings and draws upon mythology, dream-narratives, Shakespeare, G.K. Chesterton, and other highbrow literary influences. I personally consider it a meandering mess with nothing really original to say. But it has enough fans who would argue that it's really a classic, so maybe I'm just missing something about its supposed brilliance.
I'll declare it part of the fantasy/superhero genre, because it has a lot of those elements within it. Lowbrow trash in that genre is pretty much the default. Picking a genuine CLASSIC is something that would be controversial because creators have only been taking the medium seriously for a few decades and that's arguably not enough time to really separate the wheat from the chaff yet; I don't want to just project my own tastes onto these labels. So I'll avoid comics for this homework...

IN THE END, I'm still hemming and hawing over what's what. But it has my gears turning and I consider that to be a good thing!
(And I hope this long, meandering comment isn't too meaningless. Just want to share some of my thought-process.)

aaa said...
Wow, this is turning into a great discussion. Just for fun, here's my 1 second throwaway version of the homework assignment. I continue to insist that brow status does not correlate in any consistent way with aesthetic quality, and that both of those are subjective. To try to demonstrate that point (at least to someone with similar tastes to mine), I picked songs, all of which I think are either good or great.

---

Highbrow: God Only Knows [tune that made McCartney jealous]
Lowbrow: Kokomo [cheezy but it sounds soo good]
Classic: Good Vibrations [Ah yes!]
[Brian-Wilson-writing-songs-while-on-acid bonus]: California Girls

---

Highbrow: Rocky Raccoon [hipster favorite, thus pretentious. awesome song]
Lowbrow: Imagine [direct to the point of being blunt. awesome song]
Classic: I'm only sleeping [straightforward, but... for me, sublime]

https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/montecookgames/invisible-sun

Let’s be upfront, though. Invisible Sun is not a game for everyone. Not because it’s difficult, but because it’s involved. It’s not really designed for casual, fire-and-forget sorts of play. It is character-focused the way a good novel or television series is character-focused, with individual story arcs, deep development, complex motivations, nonlinear narratives, and asymmetrical play. If you’re the kind of player who enjoys musing over your character between sessions, thinking deeply about the setting and events in the game, and making interesting choices, then Invisible Sun is the game you’ve been waiting for all this time.

There's even an app (an app!) for playing side sessions of the game with individual players in your spare time.

John Michael Greer said...
Jbucks, the only artists who got by when Rome was falling were those who made pretty things they could sell to barbarian warlords once those settled down and stopped just taking stuff, and those who found a niche making art of various kinds for the church. As for me, the fiction I like to write is genre fiction -- science fiction and fantasy -- which generally counts as lowbrow if not trash, and so can count on an audience long after highbrow novels are being used to prop up half-broken sofas.

Glad to hear about your transition to piano, btw! I'm in the process of doing the same thing -- I've been promised a secondhand piano from a home that needs to downsize. Up to now I've been playing a surplus electric parlor organ, the classic 1970s spinet variety with two offcenter keyboards and one octave of foot pedals; that's the standard instrument in Masonic lodges, and almost nobody knows how to play them any more, so I may be setting myself up for a long if not lucrative career as lodge musician. ;-) But I'd be happier playing something that didn't have to be plugged in.

Unknown Eagle, I've done that in the past with great results, and will do it again the next time I'm somewhere within a hundred miles of an Indian restaurant. Those are kind of few and far between in the north central Appalachians!

Yucca, so noted. Of course it will want to be handled with a light touch, and with a sense of many options -- it simply strikes me that there will be paintings on walls in various places we've visited, statues in public places, and the like, and I neglected that in the first-draft episodes.

Bill, that's *really* funny. Thank you.

Sven, good. A movement into abstraction doesn't have to be a Nay-saying, of course -- the Overman can dance with abstractions all night -- but the current form thereof, no question, it's a flight from life into a realm of artificialities. Definitely the sort of thing that needs to be clobbered with a very, very big hammer. (Or was that supposed to be a battle-ax?)

Alvin, you've got me -- I went looking for such a post on Galabes and couldn't find it, while here, there are currently 513 posts to search through and that's a bit much even for my recollections!

Cherokee, actually, there was -- the Dark Ages in particular saw a lot of abstract and semiabstract forms. Its excesses were somewhat constrained by the fact that barbarian warlords had definite tastes in such things. ;-) I'm glad to hear you have art on your walls -- and that it reflects your taste and your wife's, not what curators think you ought to enjoy!

Patricia, yes, and in a couple of generations the only classical music that survives is the stuff that can be played simply and unpretentiously to delight a casual audience. That's my point.

David, funny. A nice bit of worldbuilding byplay.

Paulcito, nah, you don't get credit for the assignment unless you also have trash of the same genre to compare it to.

Juhana, as far as I know, it's only available directly from the publisher as yet. Once the trade paper edition comes out, that may change.

Alex, thanks for the link!

JoAnna, don't forget the other side of the picture. The highbrow music was being composed and performed for rich patrons, but there were also dances being played at peasant weddings and wandering bards going from household to household, playing and singing old songs in exchange for a place to sleep and a couple of square meals. Here in the US, Appalachian folk used to have their own musical repertoire, played on cheap fiddles and mountain dulcimers, and those weren't nohow played for rich folks, no sirree. That's likely to come back as cheap electronic music fades into memory.

John Michael Greer said...
Dennis, as noted above, art is the manufacture of collectibles for the rich. Maseratis in garages certainly count.

Jeff(back)inWa, thank you! Good to see some bits of common sense beginning to trickle through the online noise...

Pinku-sensei, duly noted. And of course it was driven by yet another transfer of things of value from the wage class to the salary class...

Nancy Sutton, why does this post make you sad? It's certainly not about telling you what you should or should not like; it's about encouraging you to like what you like and dislike what you dislike, without letting some self-proclaimed expert tell you that you have to like dog barf or you don't understand art. Does that upset you, and if so, why?

Shane, is that the collapse of Christianity, or the collapse of a particular kind of Puritan cultural hangover that's been mistaken for Christianity for too long?

AT, thank you. I agree that communication is an essential part of art; if art does not communicate it fails, and if it deliberately avoids communication -- why, then it's dog barf of the Borborygmist school, no question.

LatheChuck, in fact, you're exactly the kind of patron who got art through the last set of dark ages. It was churches, supported by the tithes of their parishioners, who provided very nearly the sole venue for most arts (everything but fabric arts, jewelry, poetry, and a few kinds of music) from the fall of Rome to the dawning of the High Middle Ages.

Zach, so noted. As I pointed out above, it was meant in large part as a counterweight to the very common caricature within the art world that portrays everybody outside that world as a flatulent philistine who hates art. It is also, I think, justified in just enough cases to make it useful as a challenge; in the case of this artwork, how do I know that it's not just dog barf with a pretentious title tacked onto it?

Monica, good! But you're missing the absolutely classic lowbrow trash in the field of astrology, the newspaper sun sign astrology column -- the Thomas Kincade of the science of the stars. (Though I grant Linda Goodman honorable mention.) In terms of classic writers, I'm personally partial to Llewellyn George and Alan Leo -- possibly worth a glance if you have the opportunity.

Forever Becoming, that's a completely different world, and yes, it follows very different patterns. One of the things Oswald Spengler points out is that tribal societies don't go through the great cycles that affect and afflict civilizations, and so they can keep on working with the same motifs and ideas for thousands of years and never run out of notional space. I suppose one way to think of it is that they reached the stage of performance early on, and never saw any reason to leave it.

Violet, excellent. Yes, that works.

Ganv, not everything lowbrow is trash, nor is everything highbrow necessarily trash; the trash label belongs to the extremes, with kitsch and warhol at the ultimate ends of the curve. Again, if you want scientific warhol, I really think the latest rounds of string theory are the place to look.

Pinku-Sensei, we were all waiting for you to do it. ;-)

Unknown, thank you! That's really an elegant application of the ternary.

Arkady, no, it would just mean the emperor was a different kind of fool, and didn't mind being seen in his underwear by most of his subjects.

nuku said...
JMG,
Movies are my favorite art form, so

For low-brow trash I nominate, among the hundreds of thousands of examples, 1976‘s “Rocky“ staring Slyvester Stallone. It had tremendous “feel-good” appeal to the working class being a classic “rags to riches” story set against the backdrop of the American Dream: perseverance, hard work, following your dream, a good woman who believes in you, and a bit of luck will get you success no matter what your class or ethnic background. An utterly predictable plot, and even the profession of the main character, boxing, is at the low-brow end of the world of Sport.

For high-brow trash I nominate the 1973‘s “The Holy Mountain” by Alejandro Jodorowsky. One possible description of the plot is: “A Christlike figure wanders through bizarre, grotesque scenarios filled with religious and sacrilegious imagery. He meets a mystical guide who introduces him to seven wealthy and powerful people, each representing a planet in the Solar system. These seven, along with the protagonist, the guide and the guide's assistant, divest themselves of their worldly goods and form a group of nine who will seek the Holy Mountain, in order to displace the gods who live there and become immortal“. I saw it at UC Berkeley when it first came out, and it was a real hit with the arty crowd who would project all sorts of personal meaning into it (a bit like abstract art!).

For classic I nominate 1957’s “12 Angry Men“ by Sidney Lumet staring Henry Fonda. This is a straightforward B&W film set in a single room on a hot day with a jury trying to decide the guilt or innocence of a young man accused of murdering his father. Its all about how the phrase “presumed innocent before proven guilty“ actually works out among 12 average men, each of whom brings their own prejudices and preconceptions to the deliberation. Its about Justice, fair play, the trial by jury system, and about how one man who acknowledges his own doubts can influence the other 11 to explore what starts out to be an “open and shut” case. Fantastic naturalistic acting, no gimmicks, and a solid logical tight script. Not preachy and not obvious how its going to end. To me its a classic both as a well crafted movie and as a brilliant piece of social commentary.

Peter VE said...
Barnaby Evans created WaterFire (http://waterfire.org/) specifically in contrast to the inside snobbery of the art establishment. One of his favorite anecdotes was being confronted by a construction worker who thanked Mr. Evans for the enjoyment his family had at the previous Fire, and that it had been the first time they had all been together without arguing for the whole evening. The final thing he said was that “it's almost like its art or something”. That says all that need be said about the art establishment.

Matthias Gralle said...
Too late to get a response, probably, but I just wanted to add my personal experiences with "difficult" art. I tried to read several of Doris Lessing's Canopus works as a teenager, simply because I read all fantasy/SF I could get my hands on, and my feeling was that she consciously strove to make her prose disagreeable. I liked to read about the happenings, but hated the way they were transmitted. Now this was in a German translation, so some of the fault may be the translator's.

Many other works of art I found very hard going, but glimpsed a little bit of beauty here and there, and that encouraged me to go on and struggle till I saw more and more of the beauty. The Divina Commedia, the Aeneid, Moby Dick... I can say it was sincere appreciation of bits of beauty, I was not simply brainwashed into liking the classics, because I did not like book V of the Aeneid at all and in the end skipped it, and I never appreciated Homer enough to read more than a few hundred lines, in spite of trying hard to like him.

The same thing in music. I loved the first madrigal by Monteverdi I heard in a music store (Sestina, from book VI), bought the CD and heard it dozens of times until I liked 13 of the 14 tracks, and then some more until I appreciated the 14th track, and then other Monteverdi CDs.

All I mean to say is, I think one needs a small opening into the work of art, otherwise there is no way to judge if it is worth it to spend the effort to go on. Some things will inevitably require effort, but it seems preposterous on the part of the artist to consciously block every sensuous entry and make everything grating on purpose. Your only reason to dedicate yourself to understanding a work of art would then be vanity - "getting it", "being part of the in crowd".

Alvin Leong said...
JMG, no problem, thanks anyway!

I remember reading an article which dealt with that topic (a tripartite division of existence forgotten after Cartesian mind-body dualism and its relation to magic) and this is the only place I could think of where I might have read something like that. I have tried different search terms involving "descartes, cartesian, dualism" but with no luck so far.

I also tried Googling this topic, and it seems to me that almost all post-Cartesian Western philosophy follows the lines of this dichotomy -- either with a bias towards one of idealism or materialism, or just shrugging and taking its assumptions for granted. None of them actually posit a third aspect of existence distinct from mind and body.

In the Christian world, the tripartite division of body, soul and spirit is a clear version of the idea but even then I can't seem to find sources which discuss how Descartes dropped the idea of spirit completely. The sources which do discuss the Christian tripartite division don't seem interested in comparing it with Descartes or discussing its role in magic. I found a book titled "Magic, Body and the Self in Eighteenth-Century Sweden" on Google Books which seems to deal with non-Cartesian ideas of the self in Swedish folk magic but that's it.

For a Buddhist perspective, there is an interesting book called "Secret Map of the Body" by Gyalwa Yangonpa, translated by Elio Guarisco, which deals with this.

HalFiore said...
After writing the previous comment, I got to thinking about the use of art for group cohesion, and an expression ran through my head: "See? I belong. I've even got the T-shirt!"

That got me thinking about the T-shirt as wearable, self-identifying art, and my attempt at the assignment took a very different direction than I had planned. So...

Highbrow trash: A shirt I have to admit I owned back in the 80s. Solid black, high quality cotton, slim-fit T with a single very small line over the left breast in a very plain, white text, "Life is hard, then you die." Bought in a trendy New Wave boutique in SF during the latter days of the punk scene there. Definitely trash, it's a T-shirt, after all, but with just enough pretentiousness to appeal to someone straddling the line between wanting social progress and a nihilist desire to burn it all down.

Lowbrow trash: A big, white, baggy shirt I saw some years later on someone I knew to be working class with garishly colored huge words splashed across the front, "Life's a BI*CH then you DIE!" Never mind the crudity of the language, which to be honest, never bothered me when it suited my purposes, but the actual message was not too subtly changed. The fact that something is hard does not mean that it is... unpleasant..., just something one must get through, and in the end, well, you're still going to die.

For classic, I'm going to have to go with the skin-tight black T's seen on European badguys and henchmen in James Bond films back in the 60s that first opened a lot of people's eyes to the idea that a T-shirt could be something beside underwear.

Now, can a T-shirt be something beside trash? I would say yes. I had a neighbor in SF in the 70s who hand screened shirts with beautiful, elegant designs from Asian art. I wish I had some of them now.

jessi thompson said...
I love hand me downs. The key is volume. Take the whole giveaway bag home and take your time going through it. Also, it's a lot easier to alter an article of clothing than it is to make something from scratch. Pants too short? Sew on an inch of lace at the botto, now you've up cycled. Stain on the collar? Maybe a button will cover it. Sew another one on the other side. Use the really hideous clothes for practice sewing darts. Or add the ugly ones to your giveaway bag n pass them on. Also, hit up goodwill. Hit the upscale parts of town to find new stuff that went out of style. Also Levis are great because they use the same cuts of jeans every decade, they literally never go out of style.

8/16/16, 11:09 PM

John Michael Greer said...
Submarine, they know they don't have to worry about Obama now; he's not going to do much of anything before the end of his term. It's his successor who's at issue, which is why the sudden emergence of a full-on military alliance between Russia, China and Iran -- with reports in overseas media suggesting that Turkey may be making overtures in that direction -- is such a huge shift. Trump's proposal to defuse hostilities with the Russians and refocus on solving our domestic problems makes a lot of sense to me, of course, whereas Clinton's confrontationalism strikes me as a very good way to drag my Twilight's Last Gleaming scenario out of the realm of fiction and onto the headlines. But we'll see.

Robert, funny. Yes, that's about right.

WB, there were equivalent parallels between the Athenian and (early) Macedonian empires. Notice the very large differences: the British Empire was a matter of direct ownership of overseas real estate, the US empire a matter of client states subject mostly to economic control by private industry; the British Empire focused above all else on the control of sea lanes, the US is uninterested in those (even though they remain a paramount strategic factor); "the third world" is a big label, and the areas in which Britain had its most important holdings (south and east Africa, India, Malaya, Australasia) are not the areas where the US has historically exercised the most control (the Middle East, Latin America, western Africa), and so on. I really do think it's two different empires.

Shane, so? Lots of artists have died messy deaths.

Compound F said...
I find your homework assignment too easy. The lowbrow trash is the Everyman's theory of reward and punishment: The beatings will continue until the morale improves! The highbrow trash is Skinner's "The Behavior of Organisms." The "classic" is Marion & Keller Breland's "The Misbehavior of Organisms." They were students of Skinner who found that his theory didn't work in practice when they tried to use the theory to make money training animals for commercial success. It's so beautiful a story that you end up with dancing chickens, miserly raccoons, and prodigal pigs.

http://psychclassics.yorku.ca/Breland/misbehavior.htm

I always love reading the Druid for often pointing out the obvious.

Dudley Dawson said...
"As for me, the fiction I like to write is genre fiction -- science fiction and fantasy -- which generally counts as lowbrow if not trash, and so can count on an audience long after highbrow novels are being used to prop up half-broken sofas."

No need to respond. I just want to say, as a fellow genre writer, hearing you say this makes me very happy. I LOL'd.

I've often whined to my wife about the future of writing and she takes a similar approach: "People want escape in hard times." I might call it fun or laughter, maybe even entertainment rather than escape, but I guess it's a horse apiece. Either way, I have a kind of frantic optimism that we might see a return to the days of ubiquitous and cheap pulp fiction. That'd be fine by me. When done well, it's pretty great stuff.

Thanks again for the great discussion this week.

Malcolm M said...
In terms of dogs, lowbrow trash is the yellow lab, ubiquitous, affectionate, always wiling to play fetch, and dumb as a box of rocks. Or the pit bull breeds, aggressive and possessive, reduced to a caricature to be brandished by aggressive young men and newly-single women. These are once-specialized working dogs bred into a puddle. They are the tie-dye and the wife-beater t-shirts of dogs.

Highbrow trash is the over-bred miniature show dog. High strung and prone to disease, Doomed to a short life. Entirely dependent on its caregiver. Useless as a pet or a working dog, it only serves to impress judges who get down on all fours and examine it's rear end for proper pucker. These dogs are scarfs once meant to warm the neck, bred into silk ascots that get caught in machinery. It is the puffy shirt of dogs.

When it comes to highbrow, the term "crawling up their own *-holes" comes to mind.

The elitist insider nature of highbrow trash reminds me of what happens when a group of friends share movie quotes or inside jokes, eventually it devolves into shorthand, and the players can communicate entire references with grunts and hand gestures. It's like the joke about the numbered jokes, except the players all start throwing random numbers around and still act like it's funny.

Or, in the concept of simulacra, lowbrow is first-order simulacra that has become self-parody, and highbrow is fourth-order simulacra on steroids.

8/19/16, 7:01 AM

DoubtingThomas said...
@JMG: Happy to say I can't do that homework..

Classic, lowbrow or highbrow doesn't work for me as a classification system.

Such things for me imply either accepting the imposition of someone else's classification system or imposing my own on others and fosters the creation of division, possibly snobbery while also providing ego-strokes.

I like what I like and have an aversion to 'names' / 'labels' of the emotive/judgemental variety.
(Whether that be in the form of populist "labels" on art / clothes / etc ..)

It was interesting that people felt threatened enough by a throw away line in a fictional story to attempt to aggressively "correct" you. That behaviour I find fascinating.

About This Archive

This archive contains the posts and comments from John Michael Greer's blog, The Archdruid Report, from May 2006 to June 2017.

Follow Greer's current writing on his new blog, www.ecosophia.net, or on his Dreamwidth account, ecosophia.dreamwidth.org.

The scripts used to produce this archive are hosted on GitHub. A downloadable copy of the archive is available on Google Drive.

Contact the archive creator at squirrel2038 at gmail.com.

About JMG

John Michael Greer is Past Grand Archdruid of the Ancient Order of Druids in America , current head of the Druidical Order of the Golden Dawn, and the author of more than thirty books on a wide range of subjects, including peak oil and the future of industrial society. He lives in Cumberland, MD, an old red brick mill town in the north central Appalachians, with his wife Sara.

Well of Galabes

If you enjoy this blog and can handle discussions of Druidry, magic, and occult philosophy, you might like my other blog, Well of Galabes.
